Corporate News: Precious Metals Exact Toll
Corporate News: Precious Metals Exact Toll
By Dana Mattioli
624 words
16 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors have been flocking to precious metals as a safe haven, boosting prices for the likes of gold and
platinum. But that's putting a squeeze on companies that use the metals in industries as varied as
construction equipment and film making.
Precious metals tend to keep their value even as inflation and economic uncertainty hurt investments like
stocks and bonds. That has fueled a flood of investment in exchange-traded funds, which track benchmark
indexes that are backed by gold and other metals.
"There's been a lavish courtship from the ETFs into this sector," says Jon Nadler, senior analyst at bullion
dealer Kitco Metals Inc. At $1,828 a troy ounce, platinum is up more than 20% from a year ago. Many
consumer products -- including cars, consumer electronics and pharmaceuticals -- either contain platinum or
require it for processing, Mr. Nadler says.
For its cement mixers and truck cranes, Terex Corp. buys engines with emissions-control devices containing
platinum and palladium, the latter of which has nearly doubled in price from last year to $832 a troy ounce.
With its engine suppliers facing higher costs for the metals, Terex is bracing for higher prices when its
contracts are adjusted this summer, says Timothy Fiore, senior vice president of supply management for the
Westport, Conn., company.
Corning Inc. uses platinum and rhodium to line the melting tanks it uses to make glass for television sets and
other consumer electronics. The Corning, N.Y., company has stockpiled $2 billion in metals, mostly platinum
and rhodium, says company Treasurer Mark Rogus. Rhodium, which isn't traded on a commodities
exchange, has fluctuated between $2,300 and $2,925 an ounce since early last year, he says. To reduce the
risk of higher prices, Corning has entered contracts that pay around $2,300 an ounce for rhodium and
between $1,400 and $1,600 an ounce for platinum.
"Our main objective is to provide certainty of the price, rather than speculation," Mr. Rogus says. The
company could take a hit, however, if either metal's price decreases below the prices Corning has locked in.
"We'd love to get it cheap, but we're under no illusion that we can beat the market," he says.
Corning's researchers have been exploring ways to rely less on the metals or find substitute materials, but
they haven't had any luck, Mr. Rogus says.
The company has ruled out raising prices to its customers, who are also squeezed by higher costs and
consumer skittishness.
Silver prices have soared to more than $30 an ounce from $18 a troy ounce in the last year. Though the
world has roughly a 5,000-ton surplus of silver, prices for the metal closed last year at their highest level in
three decades, says Suki Cooper, a precious-metals analyst with Barclays Capital.
That is pinching the film industry. For every dollar increase in silver's price per ounce, Eastman Kodak Co.'s
earnings are hurt between $10 million and $15 million, says Brad Kruchten, president of the Rochester, N.Y.,
company's film group.
Kodak this year started indexing the price of its film to silver prices. "Customers in general don't like that,"
Chief Executive Antonio Perez told investors earlier this month. But he said customers understand the
rationale.
Kodak also continues to enter hedging contracts on silver -- essentially, insuring against sharp price
increases -- and it is working to shift toward digital services.
Fujifilm Holdings Corp. raised prices 10% on its silver-halide photographic paper and print materials in the
U.S. The company also increased prices between 5% and 20% world-wide on photographic paper.

Forex View: Skies Darken for the Euro --- Dollar, Swiss Franc, Yen Set to Gain as Havens Currencies
Currency Trading
Forex View: Skies Darken for the Euro --- Dollar, Swiss Franc, Yen Set to Gain as Havens Currencies
By Stephen L. Bernard
575 words
14 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- All signs point to dollar strength and euro weakness in the coming days as an array of issues
from the future of Egypt to euro-zone sovereign debt remain unresolved.
With questions still looming about the post-Mubarak era in Egypt and the Middle East, as well as the euro
zone's persistent debt problems, haven currencies and assets could find more favor, analysts said.
The Swiss franc, Japanese yen and gold rank highly among the safety nets that, like the dollar, could attract
buying interest.
Initially, traders unwound some haven positions after Egyptian President Hosni Mubarak resigned on Friday.
But those moves quickly faded as market participants gravitated toward safer assets.
And traders widely believe that drift will continue. The euro fell to $1.3547 late Friday, more than 1% below its
highest level of the week.
"In the short run, there's probably more uncertainty than less" now that Mr. Mubarak is gone, said Aroop
Chatterjee, chief foreign-exchange quantitative strategist at Barclays Capital. "It's not clear when elections will
happen. It's not clear really who is in control."
The lack of answers, Mr. Chatterjee said, makes the dollar and other havens more appealing investments
right now. "You would have to be long in safe-haven currencies," Mr. Chatterjee said. The euro is considered
a riskier asset, so it typically declines at times of risk aversion.
Not only will the transition of power be a key question for traders, the potential spillover effects on the region
are a major uncertainty, as well.
"Does this fire jump the burn line and set ablaze a Gulf oil producer?" said David Gilmore a partner at Foreign
Exchange Analytics in Essex, Conn. Mr. Gilmore said that if it did, it would pose serious problems for the
global economy because of the potential impact on oil prices. If the issue remains contained to Egypt, the
effect on financial markets could be minimal, he said.
Uncertainty about the Middle East could last indefinitely. Yet markets are adept at adapting.
"Markets get used to crisis," said Axel Merk, chief Investment officer of Merk Investments in Palo Alto, Calif.
"The first time it happens, it rocks the world," Mr. Merk said of crises.
But each subsequent follow-up shock has a smaller effect on the market.
Diminished concern over Egypt could allow other topics to have a bigger impact on currencies. Analysts said
European sovereign debt-concerns are like to emerge once more as a determining factor in trading.
The euro was hurt recently and is likely to continue to struggle because of "policy makers' inability to agree on
any measures to resolve debt problems," said Omer Esiner, chief market analyst at Commonwealth Foreign
Exchange in Washington.
Sovereign-debt yields in some of the euro zone's weaker countries, like Portugal and Spain, will be closely
watched to see how traders feel about the 17-nation bloc's debt situation. If yields on Portuguese or Spanish
debt rise, that would show investors aren't confident in these countries' ability to improve their economies and
manage their deficits, which would be negative for the euro and benefit the dollar.
Analysts said the European Central Bank recently entered the market to buy Portuguese debt, a sign that
worries about the fiscal health of the euro zone are resurfacing.

OFF DUTY --- Style & Fashion: Menswear's Young Bloods Kick It Old School --- A crop of burgeoning new designers return to classic, quiet...
OFF DUTY --- Style & Fashion: Menswear's Young Bloods Kick It Old School --- A crop of burgeoning
new designers return to classic, quiet understatement
By Edward Helmore
1,516 words
12 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
D4
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
'There's a big difference between men's fashion and menswear," said Paul Trible, the 30-year-old owner of
the Newport News, Va.-based shirting company, Ledbury. "Most men are somewhat nervous about being
labeled fashionable." This sentiment is one shared by a crop of up-and-coming designers producing
trend-allergic clothing that's all about fit, quality and, above all, understatement. Before launching his line, Mr.
Trible lived in London, working at an international aid agency, where he gained an appreciation for the codes
of British shirt making. His firm now sells an average of 1,500 of their distinctive Anglo-American cutaway
collar shirts monthly. "Under the menswear umbrella you can do things with fit and color -- nothing too
outrageous -- but still respect the way men want to dress," Mr. Trible said.
When Kirk Miller, 32, started his nine-month-old made-to-measure label, Miller's Oath, he looked to his family
roots for inspiration. In the 19th century, the Miller family sold dry goods to gold rush miners in Groton, South
Dakota -- a heritage he hints at with his choice of emblem: a pick-axe. His small store west of New York's
SoHo neighborhood is a testament to his rare eye for cut and fabric -- his wares recently won him a
nomination for best menswear designer in America from GQ -- but also to the discreetly booming business of
bespoke. His jackets echo the shape of a dandy tailcoat, and Mr. Miller's shirts have an unusual cutaway
collar reminiscent of a bank teller in a Sergio Leone Western. "I love the idea of history, but this isn't a general
store from 1895. I like to keep my clothes elegant and timeless but also like to play with proportions in an
updated sense," he said. While Miller's operation is highly specialized and utilizes local New York
manufacturing -- his suits run to around $3,000 -- many of the new tailors are looking to create volume
businesses by scaling up production as well as minimizing costs with foreign manufacturing.
The big players in this fast-bespoke market include David Shottenstein's Astor and Black. Wildly popular with
professional athletes, the label had so many clients playing in last Sunday's Super Bowl, Mr. Schottenstein
couldn't pick a team to support. But this isn't your father's style of tailor, asking discreetly if you dress left or
right -- it's suiting that's technology dependent and rapidly produced and delivered. Established in 2004 when
Mr. Shottenstein was just 22, the company is now a $20 million business boasting testimonials from Oracle's
Larry Ellison and hook-ups with Bentley, who offer customers a bespoke Astor and Black suit, shirt and tie
with the purchase of a car. Now 21, Mr. Schottenstein says the growing demand for custom made clothes --
which has, surprisingly, increased since the economic downturn -- has lead to manufacturing innovations that
have produced savings that can be passed on to the customer. "When economic times are challenging, any
advantage is useful," Mr. Schottenstein said. "Impressions are everything. When you walk into a meeting you
should be put together nicely."
Many of this new breed of tailor are also looking to bring the exclusivity of bespoke to a broader market.
Peyton Jenkins, one of the two founders of Manhattan-based Alton Lane, says a well-fitting suit is a business
necessity. With the air of old-world Southern charm, the Flatiron district showroom offers shots of bourbon to
visitors, along with the more contemporary option to step into a 3D body scanner. "Our customers want to
look the part at work and they're concerned with finding clothes that fit," Mr. Jenkins said. "It used to be that if
you spent too much time putting yourself together, eyebrows would be raised. Now it has gone the other way
-- if you don't make an effort, it's a reflection of how you take care of yourself." Mr. Jenkins and his partner
Colin Hunter saw an opportunity in the market for the man who doesn't want to drop thousands of dollars at a
department store but still wants a tailored suit. By keeping costs low -- taking body measurements with the
scanner, manufacturing in Thailand, marketing online and maintaining minimum mark-ups -- Alton Lane offers
bespoke shirts at $150 and suits at $485. And the business is growing: Last year, Alton Lane turned over $1
million in orders, much of it from repeat online customers.
Yet as fast as menswear moves toward computer imaging and overseas production, other designers remain
rooted in the tradition of creating high-cost, low-volume wares with a greater emphasis on individuality and
artistry. "We've seen a determined swing back to tailored clothes and a more grown-up style of dressing,"
observed Patrick Grant, owner of the Savile Row tailor Norton & Sons and the off-the-peg line E. Tautz.
"People seem fed-up with dressing casually, and they're discovering the joys of putting on well-made

--- Page 121 ---
Page 121 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
clothes." E. Tautz was started essentially from scratch (it had once been a sporting tailor founded in the
1860s), but Norton & Sons was a venerable Savile Row tailor about to go out of business when Mr. Grant,
now 38, raised capital through the sale of his own house to acquire and revive the business. After barely four
years, the former material science graduate has turned Norton & Sons into a highly-starred name in British
bespoke tailoring that produces around 250 suits a year at $5,000 a piece. His clients include everyone from
European aristocracy to British fashion designers and models. "People want the simplest most understated
clothes they can imagine, beautifully cut and fitted with no hint of flamboyance at all," Mr. Grant explained.
"Cloth and execution are key." It's an ideal, he says, that dates back to Beau Brummell, the British dandy of
the Regency period who made the modern suit and tie fashionable for men. Although he advocated polishing
his boots with Champagne he maintained (without apparent contradiction) that men should be unremarkably
dressed.
Johnson & Johnson heir Jamie Johnson and Ralph Lauren's nephew Greg Lauren have entered the
menswear market with designs that echo or play on their backgrounds. Neither Mr. Johnson's rakish living
jackets and dressing-gowns nor Mr. Lauren's re-interpretation of his uncle's brand of style iconography will
likely make it to a mall near you, and neither come cheap. Mr. Johnson's Black Sweater line (named for the
legend of a hostess on Florida's Jupiter Island who would place a black sweater on the porch of any visitor
she disapproved of) is stocked at Bergdorf Goodman; while Mr. Lauren's one-of-a-kind deconstructed
tuxedos and field jackets made from vintage military duffels, army tents and sailor uniforms are at Barneys in
New York. "We've been trained to wear clothes with a certain kind of strong macho aesthetic," Mr. Lauren
explained. "I wanted to ask, 'Are you man enough to wear a pleated, falling-apart tuxedo?' " Mr. Lauren, who
was an artist before he launched his eponymous line, first learned to sew cutting life-size tuxedos and
three-piece suits out of paper for an exhibition he did in Los Angeles titled "Alteration," which explored the
construction of iconic menswear garments.
Mr. Johnson, who has made several documentaries about American aristocratic life including "Born Rich,"
references specific aspects of luxury living. His tan-and-black reversible dressing gown, he hopes, evokes a
sense of the good life; the slightly over-length raincoats and jackets resemble equestrian tailoring. "I find the
idiosyncracies of upper-class life amusing and I want to incorporate and comment on that through clothing,"
he said. "Everybody's saying something in the clothes they choose to wear so I guess I'm joining that
tradition."
Mr. Lauren and Mr. Johnson's forays into fashion speak to a menswear market that outperformed other areas
of the garment industry in the last three months of 2010. That could be women buying their menfolk
Christmas presents, or it may point to an evolution in the way men shop and deport themselves. Later this
month, Natalie Massenet, founder of the shopping website Net-a-Porter, will launch the much buzzed about
Mr. Porter, an e-commerce destination specializing in traditional men's clothes and accessories, which will not
feature runway images as it's sister site does. It's a smart (and deliberate) strategy -- after all, most men are
reluctant shoppers and tend to shy away from what's new. "That's okay for E. Tautz," said Mr. Norton, who's
brand is sold on the site. "But it wouldn't work for Norton & Sons. We like to have a conversation with each
customer, try to understand his lifestyle and build a wardrobe for him. But we don't design the suits.
Essentially you do."

Earnings: Rio Tinto Nearly Triples Net
Earnings: Rio Tinto Nearly Triples Net
By David Fickling
859 words
11 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SYDNEY -- Rio Tinto PLC said it nearly tripled its annual net profit Thursday and that it will have a $5 billion
share buyback, but the mining giant's dramatic return to form was tempered with a warning about a world
economy that is emerging from the financial crisis.
Net profit at Rio Tinto, the world's third-biggest miner by production, grew to $14.32 billion in 2010 from $4.87
billion the previous year after record iron-ore prices, but Chief Executive Tom Albanese said emerging
markets may struggle to keep control of their economies, while developed governments could make missteps
as they pull back from emergency measures imposed during the global financial crisis.
The strong figures are a return to form for Rio Tinto following the company's $44 billion takeover of Alcan Inc.,
which left it struggling under a mountain of debt on the eve of the financial crisis and facing a takeover offer
from larger rival BHP Billiton.
"GDP growth in emerging markets and supply constraints mean the general market and pricing outlook for
commodities remain positive, albeit with elevated risk," Mr. Albanese said in a statement.
"In particular, the timing and speed at which post-global financial crisis stimulus packages are removed have
the potential to generate both volatility and substantial swings in commodity prices."
China's central bank on Tuesday raised lending and deposit rates by 0.25 percentage point as part of efforts
to cool its economy and tame inflation. Analysts are expecting China -- the world's largest steelmaker -- to
tighten monetary policy even further over the year ahead, while India, Brazil and even the U.K. are also
facing pressure to raise interest rates.
Booming commodities prices over the past year helped Rio Tinto pay down debt, invest heavily in expansion
projects and promise a return of cash to shareholders, with management committing $5 billion to a share
buyback by the end of 2012.
Copper, iron ore, tin and gold are all close to records, while silver and palladium are at multi-decade highs.
Aluminum, nickel, platinum and coal have risen to their highest levels since the global financial crisis.
Iron ore accounts for roughly two-thirds of the company's net profit after tax, with copper the second-biggest
driver, accounting for one-sixth of profits.
However, the company sounded a note of caution over the medium-term outlook. Its $5 billion share buyback
was less than the figure of as much as $15 billon tipped by analysts and underlined its reluctance to pursue
large acquisitions following its takeover of Alcan.
The share buyback comes at a time when shareholders have been pressuring mining companies to start
giving back some of their returns from record commodity prices.
Rio Tinto Chief Financial Officer Guy Elliott said he had "never heard anything like" the $15 billion figure from
shareholders but said Rio had to continue to conserve cash due to the uncertain economic environment.
"We do want a prudent balance sheet because the outlook is volatile," he said. The firm is keen to pursue a
single-A credit rating from rating agency Standard & Poor's, which would match a similar rating from Moody's
and lower its borrowing costs.
Rio also declared a final dividend of $1.08, compared with 45 cents the previous year. Xstrata PLC on
Tuesday tripled its dividend, while BHP Billiton Ltd.'s largest shareholder Blackrock Inc. has repeatedly
pressed the miner to increase its cash payouts.

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Cash flow grew by 70% over the year, coming to $23.53 billion, compared with $13.83 billion the previous
year. That helped reduce net debt to $4.3 billion, compared with $12 billion at June 30 last year.
On the underlying basis preferred by equity analysts, net profit came to $13.99 billion in the year ended Dec.
31, compared with the previous year's $6.29 billion. Underlying net profit strips out one-off items and
accounting and tax adjustments that can distort the true picture of a company's performance.
The median of analyst estimates for underlying net profit was $14.1 billion, with forecasts ranging from $12.1
billion to $15.1 billion, according to Merrill Lynch.
Since its half-year results last year, Rio Tinto has announced aggressive plans to build up its operations and
in-the-ground resources world-wide, with plans to spend $12 billion by the end of 2011 to shore up its position
in the global iron-ore market. Around $8.1 billion has been dedicated to the iron-rich Pilbara region of
Western Australia alone, with plans to increase output in the region to 333 million tons in 2015 from 185
million tons last year.
Mr. Albanese warned that no miner should be too dependent on any one commodity. "We certainly are
benefiting from record iron-ore production at a time of record iron ore prices, but these aren't necessarily
permanent situations," he said.
"Look back 10 years and ask what were the most uniformly bearish commodities on a consensus basis, and
they would be the ones that are most uniformly bullish today."

Crude Spread Hits Record as Oil Slides
Commodities Report
Crude Spread Hits Record as Oil Slides
By Dan Strumpf
373 words
9 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Oil futures edged lower as fears of supply disruptions at the Suez Canal eased, while the U.S.
benchmark oil contract traded at a record discount to its European counterpart.
Light, sweet crude for March delivery settled down 54 cents, or 0.6%, at $86.94 a barrel on the New York
Mercantile Exchange. The contract settled at a discount of $12.98 to Brent crude, which ended higher on the
ICE futures exchange.
Nymex crude earlier jumped to an intraday high of $88.11 a barrel after an unconfirmed report, originating
from an Egypt-based website, that some workers affiliated with the Suez Canal had begun a sit-in. However,
several shipping companies said operations at the canal, a major energy-transit point, continue to operate
normally, and crude prices later gave up their gains.
Nymex crude's discount to the Brent contract widened to a record level of $13.05 during intraday trading.
Concerns persist that inventory data due this week would show another increase in oil stockpiles at the
Nymex delivery point of Cushing, Okla.
Typically, the two contracts trade within $2 of each other, but rising inventories at Cushing have kept the price
difference, known as the "spread," at unusually wide levels for more than a month. The magnitude of the
spread has caused some market participants to question the Nymex contract's usefulness as a gauge of
global supply and demand for oil.
Unlike the Nymex contract, the Brent contract isn't tied to supply levels at a single location. Brent crude
settled 67 cents, or 0.7%, higher at $99.92 a barrel.
"It's never been this high and never for this long," said Andy Lipow, president of Lipow Oil Associates, of the
price gap.
Nymex futures also were pressured by the Chinese central bank's decision to raise two key interest rates.
Worries that Beijing's efforts to cool its economy will crimp demand for commodities initially sent most prices
lower, but a weaker dollar later boosted copper to a settlement roughly unchanged from Monday. Gold
futures finished at nearly a three-week high as the interest-rate increase underscored global inflation fears.

Corporate News: Offshore Drillers To Merge
Corporate News: Offshore Drillers To Merge
By Dana Cimilluca and Guy Chazan
627 words
8 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Ensco PLC agreed to buy Pride International Inc. for $7.3 billion in cash and stock, in a transaction that would
accelerate the reshaping of the offshore oil-drilling industry nearly a year after BP PLC's disastrous spill in the
Gulf of Mexico.
The two companies said Monday the deal would give Pride shareholders 0.4778 of a share in London-based
Ensco plus $15.60 in cash for each Pride share they hold. That equates to $41.60 a share, or a 21% premium
to Pride's closing price Friday on the New York Stock Exchange.
The deal, which requires shareholder approval, will close as soon as next quarter, the companies said.
In 4 p.m. composite trading on the Big Board Monday, Pride shares were up 16% at $39.80.
The deal would create the world's second-largest offshore driller, after Transocean Ltd., with 74 rigs in some
of the world's most prolific oil basins. More than a quarter of them would be deepwater rigs capable of drilling
in depths of 4,500 feet or more. Such rigs are in short supply, and command the highest rates in the industry.
Pride had been exploring a sale of the Houston-based company since late last year, and held talks with a
number of possible suitors, including Seadrill Ltd., the big Norwegian drilling contractor. Seadrill owns a
roughly 9% stake in Pride after an abortive attempt to buy the whole company in 2008. People familiar with
the matter said in November that Seadrill had gotten fed up with the minority stake and either wanted to buy
the rest of Pride or cash out.
Seadrill reacted coolly to the terms of Pride's deal with Ensco. "It's an interesting offer, but it's too early to
draw conclusions," said Esa Ikaheimonen, Seadrill's chief financial officer. "For the moment, we're keeping
our options open."
Pride is well established in Brazil and West Africa, the two fastest-growing deepwater markets, while Ensco
has a big presence in the North Sea, Southeast Asia, North America and the Middle East.
In a statement, Ensco Chairman and Chief Executive Dan Rabun, who will hold the same roles at the
combined company, called the tie-up an "ideal strategic fit, as our rig types, markets, customers and expertise
complement each other with minimal overlap."
The deal would leave current Pride stockholders with about 38% of Ensco's equity. Ensco's eight directors
would stay in place and be joined by two Pride directors.
The offshore drilling industry has come under pressure since the Deepwater Horizon disaster, when a drilling
rig leased by BP exploded in the Gulf of Mexico, killing 11 men and setting off the worst offshore oil spill in
U.S. history.
New, tighter regulations in the U.S. are expected to benefit companies with modern fleets of deepwater rigs,
like Transocean, which owned the Deepwater Horizon, Pride and Seadrill. The regulations also could make it
harder for smaller drilling contractors to compete.
Since the spill, energy-exploration companies have favored the newest drilling rigs with the strongest blowout
preventers. Industry officials say executives who contract these rigs, which can fetch rates as high as
$400,000 a day, are willing to pay more for those with the most up-to-date equipment.
The Ensco-Pride deal follows a string of smaller transactions over the past year or so. Last year, Seadrill
acquired Scorpion Offshore Ltd., Noble Corp. bought Frontier Drilling and Rowan Cos. snapped up Norway's
Skeie Drilling. But the Ensco-Pride tie-up is the industry's largest since Transocean acquired GlobalSantaFe
Corp. in 2007.

J.P. Morgan Will Accept Gold as Type Of Collateral
J.P. Morgan Will Accept Gold as Type Of Collateral
By Carolyn Cui and Rhiannon Hoyle
805 words
8 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold hasn't reinvented itself as a currency yet. But it is getting closer.
J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. said it will allow clients to use the metal as collateral in some transactions. For
example, a hedge fund wanting to borrow money for a short period can put up gold as collateral and use the
borrowings to invest elsewhere, betting on making a better return. Typically, banks accept only Treasury
bonds and stocks in such agreements.
By making the announcement, J.P. Morgan is effectively saying gold is as rock solid an investment as
triple-A-rated Treasurys, adding to a movement that places gold at the top tier of asset classes. It also is
trying to capitalize on all the gold now owned by hedge funds and private investors that is sitting idle in
warehouses.
"It's solidifying a trend that gold is re-establishing its role as a monetary and financial asset," said Carlos
Sanchez, associate director of research with New York commodities consultancy CPM Group.
J.P. Morgan said it is responding to demand from clients, many of which also store gold in the bank's vaults.
"Many clients are holding gold on their balance sheets as an inflation hedge and are looking to make these
assets work for them as collateral," said John Rivett, collateral-management executive at J.P. Morgan
Worldwide Securities Services.
J.P. Morgan's decision Monday reignited debate among gold's fans and detractors. For decades, supporters
have argued gold is a monetary asset and should be treated on an equal footing with cash. However, gold
critics argued the market has been too volatile and too small for it to be considered a legitimate currency.
Recently, though, gold's status has been rising.
Exchanges in New York, Chicago and Europe recently agreed to accept gold as collateral for certain trades.
And the World Gold Council also is gaining traction in its push to have the Basel Committee on Banking
Supervision accept the precious metal as a Tier-1 asset for banks, along with government bonds and
currencies.
In India, many financial-services companies are offering personal loans against physical gold, a market that
is expanding.
"Gold is increasingly being used as collateral around the world," said Natalie Dempster, the gold council's
director of government affairs. "All these moves reflect a growing recognition of gold's role as a high-quality,
liquid asset."
Gold futures for February delivery on Monday settled 70 cents lower, or 0.1%, at $1,347.60 a troy ounce on
the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. It reached a nominal record close on Jan. 3 of
$1,422.60.
In essence, J.P. Morgan is creating another role for gold, which has limited use now. One of the main
laments of the metal's critics is that, once bought, the metal doesn't generate any income, compared with
interest on bonds or dividends on stocks, and mainly just sits in vaults, rising and falling in value.
"It gives another use to gold as a cash instrument," said Tom Pawlicki, an analyst at MF Global, a
commodities brokerage. Investors who hold gold, he said, "might be less likely to sell it."
Gold still is far from being the integral part of the monetary system it once was.

--- Page 128 ---
Page 128 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
After World War II, under the Bretton Woods agreement, several countries pegged their currencies to the
dollar, which in turn was fixed to the price of gold. President Richard Nixon ended the dollar-gold peg in
1971.
It is unclear whether customers need to hand over the physical bullion to J.P. Morgan or at what haircut the
metal will be pledged with the bank.
There still is risk for financial institutions in taking gold as collateral.
If prices fall sharply, along with the value of the underlying trades, the collateral value could fall short of
covering the trading positions, leaving banks scrambling for more margin to cover the losses.
In the past, worries about a lack of liquidity in the gold market have prevented banks from taking gold as
collateral. But as investors piled into the market in recent years, the market has deepened.
The market is more liquid than many government-bond markets in Europe, with daily trading volumes
normally exceeding $100 billion, according to the World Gold Council.
"When a bank, such as J.P. Morgan, is willing to extend collateral value against an asset such as physical
bullion, it shows that they are not worried about the liquidity issue if they might take the collateral over or they
have to liquidate the collateral," said Frank McGhee, head precious metals trader at Integrated Brokerage
Services, a Chicago broker.

Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- Exchange-Traded Funds: Some ETFs Boom by Getting Physical --- Funds holding gold bullion are...
Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- Exchange-Traded Funds: Some ETFs Boom by Getting
Physical --- Funds holding gold bullion are popular; But there are challenges to investing directly in
other metals and commodities
By Liam Pleven
1,068 words
7 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
R3
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The investing hordes are going in for hoarding, a concept that has appealed to military strategists, legendary
value investor Benjamin Graham and squirrels.
But as fund companies create more products that stockpile raw materials, emerging challenges suggest the
idea may come up short as an investment strategy.
Since 2004, investors have socked billions into U.S. exchange-traded funds backed by raw materials rather
than by the usual futures contracts or other paper proxies for physical commodities. As investors buy shares
in the funds, the funds typically acquire more material. The value of the shares rises and falls with the actual
commodity.
Thus far, these "physically backed" funds have focused largely on precious metals -- the largest holds gold,
but others hold silver, platinum and palladium -- and have appealed to investors who have a "greater degree
of comfort" with hard assets than stocks, bonds, futures or the like, says Graham Tuckwell, head of ETF
Securities, which runs physically backed funds.
As of the end of last year, nine physically backed U.S.-based ETFs together had amassed $77.6 billion of
assets, according to fund-tracking firm IndexUniverse.com, compared with $24 billion for futures-backed
commodity ETFs.
Physically backed funds are attractive to some because they avoid a problem with futures-based
commodities investing that has hurt returns -- the expense of selling expiring monthly contracts and then
buying new ones. The impact can be large because new contracts frequently have been more expensive in
recent years -- a phenomenon known as contango that reflects both the carrying costs and expectations of
future supply and demand.
By holding the actual goods, the physically backed funds can sidestep the contango and sidle closer to what
many investors want -- returns that move in tandem with price changes in the underlying material.
Fund operators now are trying to expand beyond precious metals. Firms such as ETF Securities and J.P.
Morgan Chase & Co. are launching similar products to hold industrial metals, such as copper and lead. But
that's where things start to get tricky, and the reasons help explain the potential limits associated with
hoarding.
Precious-metals funds often have low fees because those materials are compact and relatively inexpensive
to store. That makes it easy for fund returns to closely track underlying prices.
Stockpiling bulkier materials is more expensive. The storage fee for ETF Securities' copper fund is 1.56%,
roughly four times the total 0.40% fee for SPDR Gold Shares, known by the ticker symbol GLD, the largest
physically backed ETF.
"It costs real money to store copper," says Matt Hougan, president of ETF analytics at IndexUniverse.com. By
comparison, he says, "all the gold in GLD would fit in [a] hotel room." As of mid-January, the World Gold
Council, which sponsors GLD, said the bullion held by the ETF would form a cube slightly more than 13 feet
per side.
That also helps explain why there are as yet no similar funds backed by actual crude oil or corn in silos. The
storage would probably be even more expensive, and there could be additional complications that would
curtail returns. "Oil leaks. Mice eat corn," says Mr. Hougan.

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Such funds also could prove controversial. According to Mr. Tuckwell, a fund backed by grain is "not
necessarily a good social product," and stockpiling food on behalf of investors is "more open to political
commentary."
Any such commentary likely would revolve around the impact on prices. Many observers agree that physically
backed funds can make the raw materials they buy more expensive by providing an outlet for pent-up
demand from retail investors, who can't stockpile commodities on their own. It's hard to determine exactly
how big the price impact is, but the influence has stirred opposition.
In February 2006, when silver cost less than $10 a troy ounce, an executive at a U.S. unit of Fujifilm Holdings
Corp. wrote to the Securities and Exchange Commission, saying a fund backed by silver, a key component of
photographic film, would "mean higher product costs and lost jobs in our industry."
The SEC nonetheless gave the fund the green light. Silver prices shot up, then pulled back, but ended that
year just under $13, a sizable premium to the pre-fund price. In later years, silver prices fluctuated, but rose
sharply in 2010 and recently hovered below $30. In December, Fujifilm said prices for photographic paper
would rise about 10% early this year, and cited higher costs for raw materials.
On the other hand, stockpiles of some raw materials have long been used to guard against price shocks. The
U.S. established the Strategic Petroleum Reserve after the Arab oil embargo in the 1970s. It now holds
enough crude oil to meet domestic demand for more than a month.
Amid the Great Depression, Mr. Graham, mentor to billionaire investor Warren Buffett, touted the notion of
using a stockpile of commodities to underpin paper money, in a 1937 book titled "Storage and Stability, A
Modern Ever-normal Granary." At the time, the world still operated on the gold standard. But Mr. Graham
argued that "storage of basic commodities suggests itself as the most suitable backing for a sound and
adequate currency," and added that storage "is vested with the allied function of reasonably stabilizing price
levels."
When it comes to industrial metals, Mr. Tuckwell says commodity-backed ETFs could act as "an extra safety
valve" to curb soaring prices. Investors in such funds would notice a price spike, and "that's when they'd be
unloading it," he says.
One possible reason: Investors often view precious metals as a safe-haven investment, but they might be
more willing to part with industrial metals, which society tends to view as materials that are meant to be
consumed.
Still, Mr. Tuckwell says he has modest expectations for his firm's new funds, which will buy copper, nickel, tin,
lead and zinc. He cites storage costs as one of the reasons. "I don't expect just huge amounts of money piling
into it," he says.
---
Mr. Pleven is a staff reporter for The Wall Street Journal in New York. Email him at liam.pleven@wsj.com.

Forex View: Dollar May Tap Safe-Haven Bid from Egypt Upheaval
Currency Trading
Forex View: Dollar May Tap Safe-Haven Bid from Egypt Upheaval
By Chana R. Schoenberger
775 words
7 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Uncertainty about the outcome of Egypt's crisis may again loom large in currency markets this week, buffeting
the euro and other riskier currencies and bolstering the dollar.
While such traditional safe havens as the Swiss franc and gold have intermittently gained from investors'
concerns that social unrest could spread across the Middle East, the U.S. currency has also benefited.
Omer Esiner, chief market analyst at Commonwealth Foreign Exchange in Washington, D.C., said the
headline-grabbing Egyptian turmoil should continue to give the dollar a safe-haven edge.
But others say the euro should escape any serious dents from Egypt's troubles. Indeed, the common
currency for 17 European nations again test $1.39, as investors' renewed appetite for risk raises commodities
and equities and puts pressure on the dollar, said Michael Woolfolk, a strategist at BNY Mellon.
Sunday, as central Cairo's Tahrir Square filled for a 13th day of protests, Egyptian Vice President Omar
Suleiman met representatives of the Muslim Brotherhood, an organization the government has spent years
suppressing. But none of the other parties or youth movements that have driven the demonstrations
attended, underscoring the uncertainty over how the crisis will play out.
Any gains for the dollar from the crisis may be muted because of questions about the pace of U.S. economic
recovery. Friday's U.S. payrolls data showed a mixed employment picture.
Unusually bad weather that kept many workers home made the jobs data difficult for traders to interpret. That
sparked some debate about whether the dollar should be treated more as a safe-haven currency, given the
political crisis in Egypt, or as a riskier play given the financial woes of many U.S. state governments.
The U.S. jobs data could be "bad for the dollar" against the euro and the yen this week, given that they didn't
come down clearly on the side of strong economic growth, said Morgan Stanley currency strategist Ron
Leven.
While the unemployment rate fell sharply, confounding expectations for a small rise, the increase in the
number of nonfarm jobs on the nation's payrolls was much smaller than expected. Analysts attributed much of
that to the heavy snowfall that blanketed much of U.S. last month and kept potential employees in
hibernation.
Meanwhile, attention among Asian currencies is likely to focus once again on the yuan as the Lunar New
Year holidays end.
With no major data releases expected in the U.S. this week, traders will be watching for China's January
trade data, Analysts expect the figures to show that exports continued to rise while imports grew at a slower
pace compared to December. That could signal that China's economy is cooling; a potential warning sign for
the global economic outlook.
"If you see the China trade situation deteriorate, it could be a slight negative for the dollar," Commonwealth
Foreign Exchange's Mr. Esiner said.
Chinese markets reopen Wednesday after being shuttered for a week because of the holidays. Morgan
Stanley's Mr. Leven said he expects the tightly controlled yuan to "start crawling again" as Beijing allows it to
move in line with an appreciation rate of about 6% for the year. That could affect other commodity currencies,
which are often correlated to China's because of the country's vital importance as a buyer of commodities
world-wide, he said.

--- Page 132 ---
Page 132 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, the U.S. Treasury on Friday declined to name China or any other country as a manipulator of its
currency in the Treasury's much-awaited report on foreign-exchange policies. That eased worries, for now,
that Washington might turn the yuan's exchange rate into a contentious political issue, but trade tensions
between the two countries over the artificially weak Chinese currency are unlikely to abate any time soon.
Last week was a good one for the dollar: It gained on the euro for the ninth week in the past 13, and it
snapped a three-week losing streak on the yen.
Friday, the euro weakened to $1.3590 from $1.3635 late Thursday. The dollar strengthened to 82.20 yen
from 81.57 yen. The euro moved to 111.71 yen from 111.22 yen. The U.K. pound weakened to $1.6097 from
$1.6144. The dollar leapt to 0.9547 Swiss franc from 0.9454 franc.
The Australian dollar will be in focus as traders digest retail sales and labor-market data. The economy has
been hit hard recently by first floods and then a major cyclone that destroyed crops in Queensland.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- How to Profit From Inflation --- The Scourge of Rising Prices Hasn't Hit Home Yet, but the Underlying Signs Point to...
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- How to Profit From Inflation --- The Scourge of Rising Prices Hasn't Hit
Home Yet, but the Underlying Signs Point to Trouble Ahead; Here's What You Should Do Now
By Ben Levisohn and Jane J. Kim
2,279 words
5 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Inflation, long a sleeping giant, is finally awakening. And that could present problems -- along with
opportunities -- for investors.
A quick glance at the overall inflation numbers might suggest there is little reason to worry. The most recent
U.S. Consumer Price Index was up just 1.5% over the past year. Not only was that lower than the historical
average of about 3%, but it was uncomfortably low for Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke, who prefers
to see inflation at about 2%.
Yet it is a much different situation overseas, particularly in the developing world. In South Korea, the CPI rose
at a 4.1% clip in January from a year earlier, higher than the 3.8% estimate. In Brazil, analysts expect prices
to rise 5.6% this year, exceeding the central-bank target of 4.5%. China, meanwhile, has been boosting
interest rates and raising bank capital requirements to keep inflation, which rose to 4.6% in December, in
check.
"Emerging market economies are overheating," says Julia Coronado, chief economist for North America at
BNP Paribas in New York. "They need to slow growth or inflation will become destabilizing."
Even some developed economies are seeing rising prices. Inflation in the U.K. surged to 3.7% in December,
while the euro zone's rate climbed to 2.4% in January, the fastest rise since 2008.
Much of the uptick has been driven by commodity prices. During the past six months, oil has jumped 9%,
copper has gained 36% and silver has shot up 56%. Agricultural products have soared as well: Cotton, wheat
and soybeans have risen 100%, 24% and 42%, respectively. That's a problem because rising input prices
"work their way down the food chain to CPI," says Alan Ruskin, global head of G-10 foreign-exchange
strategy at Deutsche Bank.
Of course, the main inflation driver is usually wages -- and that isn't a factor in the U.S., where high
unemployment has kept a lid on pay for three years.
Yet there isn't a historical blueprint for the inflation scenario that seems to be unfolding now. Not only has the
global economy changed drastically since the last big inflationary run during the 1970s, but the lingering
effects of the recent debt crisis remain a wild card.
For investors, that means traditional inflation busters such as real estate and gold might not work as
expected, while other strategies might perform better.
So how should you position your portfolio? The best approach, say advisers, is to tweak asset allocations
rather than overhaul them. That involves dialing back on some kinds of bonds, stocks and commodities and
increasing holdings of others. Here's a guide:
What to Sell
-- Bonds. The price of a bond moves in the opposite direction of its yield. When inflation kicks up, interest
rates usually move higher, pressuring bond prices. Even buy-and-hold investors get hurt, because higher
inflation erodes the real value of the interest payments you receive and the principal you get back when the
bond matures.
The drop is usually most extreme in longer-dated bonds, because low yields are locked in for a longer period
of time. So inflation-wary investors should shorten the maturities of their bonds, say advisers.

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The safest bonds, especially Treasurys, are usually hardest hit, because those are the most influenced by
changes in rates -- unlike corporate bonds, whose prices also move based on credit quality. From September
1986 through September 1987, for example, as inflation moved higher, Treasurys dropped 1.2%.
It might even make sense to dial back on Treasury inflation-protected securities, whose principal and interest
payments grow alongside the CPI. That's because investors already have flooded into TIPS, driving up prices
and driving down the real, inflation-adjusted yields. A typical 10-year TIPS, for example, yields just 1.1%
after inflation, compared with an average of more than 2% in recent years.
With so little cushion, long-term TIPS carry a higher risk of loss for investors who are forced to sell before the
bonds mature. "Even if inflation is rising, you can still lose money," says Joseph Shatz, interest-rate
strategist at Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch.
-- Hard assets. Real estate may be a classic inflation hedge, but it seems likely to disappoint investors this
time around. Even though rising inflation should put upward pressure on home prices, the twin forces of
record-high foreclosures and consumers reducing their debt loads are expected to mute price gains for
several years, says Milton Ezrati, senior economist at asset manager Lord Abbett. That's a far cry from the
1970s, when the median home price rose 43%, according to U.S. Census data.
Gold is another traditional inflation hedge that might be less effective now. With prices already having more
than quadrupled over the past nine years, many strategists see substantial inflation already factored into the
price.
Historically, gold has moved with the money supply. During the last 30 years, the correlation has been about
69%, according to FactSet data. (A correlation of 100% means two indexes move in lockstep all the time; a
correlation of minus-100% means they move in perfect opposition.) Based on the money supply alone, gold
is priced 25% above where it should be, says Russ Koesterich, chief investment strategist at BlackRock Inc.'s
iShares.
-- Stocks. Equities can be a decent hedge against creeping inflation, because companies are better able to
pass off costs to customers. But when input costs suddenly jump, profit margins take a hit.
At the same time, the higher interest rates that accompany inflation prompt investors to demand more
profits for each dollar invested. As a result, price/earnings ratios tend to shrivel. Over the past 55 years, the
average trailing P/E ratio of a stock in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index has fallen to 16.95 during
periods with inflation running between 3% and 5%, from 19.24 during periods with inflation of 1% to 3%, the
most common inflation range since 1955, Mr. Koesterich says.
Sectors that are sensitive to interest rates, including financials, utility stocks and consumer staples, are
especially vulnerable, say advisers.
What to Buy
-- Cash and bank products. Money-market mutual funds are more attractive in inflationary environments
because the funds invest in short-term securities that mature every 30 to 40 days, and therefore can pass
through higher rates quickly. In an extreme example, money-funds posted yields over 15% during the
inflation-ravaged 1970s and early 1980s, says Pete Crane of Crane Data, which tracks money funds.
A growing number of inflation-linked savings products are cropping up as well. Incapital LLC, a Chicago
investment bank, says it has seen a pickup recently in issuances of certificates of deposit designed for a
rising-rate environment. Savers, for example, can invest in a 12-year CD whose rate starts at 3% then
gradually steps up to 4.25% starting in 2015, and peaks at 5.5% starting at 2019 until the CD's maturity in
2023.
A caveat: If inflation eases and rates fall, investors could get burned, since the issuer may call the CDs and
investors would lose out on the higher rates at maturity.
-- Bonds. One way to reduce the impact of rising inflation on bond holdings is to build a bond ladder --
buying bonds that mature in, say, two, four, six, eight and 10 years. As the shorter-term bonds mature,
investors can reinvest the proceeds into longer-term bonds at higher rates.
"A bond ladder is best for someone who doesn't mind holding them for up to 10 years," says Jeff Feldman, an
adviser in Rochester, N.Y.
Highly cautious investors might prefer the I Bond, a U.S. savings bond that earns interest based on a
twice-yearly CPI adjustment. Although the current yield on I Bonds is only 0.74%, that yield is likely to move
higher on May 1, the next time the rate is adjusted. I Bonds aren't as volatile as TIPS and appeal to

--- Page 135 ---
Page 135 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
conservative, buy-and-hold investors. The interest may also be tax-free for some families for education
expenses.
More adventurous types might consider the inflation-protected government debt of other nations, which carry
higher yields along with greater risks. The SPDR DB International Government Inflation-Protected Bond
Fund is an international inflation-protected bond exchange-traded fund designed to do well if inflation in
overseas countries moves higher. The fund returned about 6.8% in 2010 and 18.5% in 2009, according to
Morningstar Inc.
-- Bank-loan funds. Another way to exploit rising inflation is through mutual funds that buy adjustable-rate
bank loans, many of which are used to finance leveraged corporate buyouts. So-called floating-rate funds are
structured so that if interest rates rise, they collect more money. During periods of rising rates, floating-rate
funds usually outperform other bond-fund categories. In 2003, for example, as investors anticipated higher
interest rates and a stronger economy, bank-loan funds gained 10.4% while short-term bond funds gained
2.5%.
Now, amid expectations of rising inflation, investors are once again flocking to these funds, pouring in about
$7.6 billion into loan funds in the fourth quarter of last year, according to Lipper Inc. -- more than double the
previous quarterly record set in 2007. The pace has accelerated this year, with investors putting in about $3.4
billion thus far.
After gaining almost 10% last year, the funds shouldn't be counted on for much price appreciation, says Craig
Russ, who co-manages $22.7 billion of floating-rate investments across three floating-rate funds and other
accounts at Eaton Vance Corp., including the Eaton Vance Floating Rate Fund. But the funds generate plenty
of income, yielding about 4% to 5% now, according to Morningstar.
Be warned: Floating-rate funds can get creamed when investors fear the underlying loans are too risky. In
2008, for example, bank-loan funds lost 29.7%, although they zoomed 41.8% in 2009, according to
Morningstar. What's more, banks are beginning to make riskier "covenant-light" loans that carry fewer
stipulations for corporate borrowers -- a sign of frothier trends in the market.
Given the potential for big swings, floating-rate funds are best viewed as a complement to -- not a
replacement for -- investors' core bond holdings. Among Morningstar's picks in this category is the Fidelity
Floating Rate High-Income Fund, among the more conservative in the category.
-- Commodities. Materials that are more closely tied to industrial or food production seem better positioned
now than gold, say advisers. The trick is to find the best investment vehicle.
The easiest way for small investors to gain exposure to most commodities is through exchange-traded funds,
many of which use futures contracts. But such funds can be dangerous because they often face "contango" --
when the price for a future delivery is higher than the current price. The result: The ETFs lose money as they
buy new contracts, even when prices are rising.
The losses can be extreme. In 2009, for instance, while the price of natural gas rose 3.4%, the United States
Natural Gas Fund lost 56.5% as a result of rolling over futures contracts.
Some firms have rolled out ETFs that aim to address the problem. One of Morningstar's picks is the U.S.
Commodity Index Fund, run by U.S. Commodity Funds LLC. The portfolio buys the seven commodities that
are most "backwardated" -- the opposite of "contango," so rolling contracts should result in a profit -- along
with the seven commodities with the most price momentum.
"USCI provides an outlet for investors who want broad commodities exposure but don't want to worry about
the daily dynamics," says Tim Strauts, a Morningstar analyst.
Other funds play inflation by holding many different assets to protect against rising prices no matter where
they show up. The IQ Real Return ETF, launched in 2009 by IndexIQ, aims to provide a return equal to the
CPI plus 2% to 3% over a two- to three-year period. To get there, it invests across a dozen or so
inflation-sensitive assets -- including currencies and commodities.
-- Stocks. One corner of the market tends to do better when prices rise suddenly: small-company value
stocks. "Because value and small stocks tend to be fairly highly [indebted] companies, inflation reduces their
liabilities," says William Bernstein of Efficient Frontier Advisors LLC, an investment-advisory firm in Eastford,
Conn.
From January 1965 through December 1980, for example, inflation averaged 6.6% a year. The Ibbotson
Small-Cap Value Index posted average annual returns of 14.4%, according to Morningstar's Ibbotson
Associates, double the S&P 500's 7.1% gain.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Morningstar's picks in the small-cap value fund category include Allianz NFJ Small Cap Value, Diamond Hill
Small Cap, Perkins Small Cap Value and Schneider Small Cap Value. Just be warned: Small value stocks
have had a good run recently, returning 134%, on average, since March 6, 2009.
In the end, the particulars of any inflation-fighting plan may not be as important as developing a plan in the
first place.
"The real problem you run into with any kind of inflation hedges," says Jay Hutchins, a financial adviser in
Lebanon, N.H., "is that if you don't already have them when inflation is around the corner, you've missed the
boat."

Unrest Pits Oil Bulls Vs. Gold bugs
Heard on the Street
Unrest Pits Oil Bulls Vs. Gold bugs
By Liam Denning
300 words
1 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
When the Middle East roars, so do oil bulls. But buying gold could be a better hedge against Egyptian
turmoil.
On its own, the threat to oil markets from further unrest in Egypt is minimal. Egypt isn't a net exporter of oil,
and less than 2% of the world's traded crude transits the Suez Canal. For that, we can thank the 1956 Suez
crisis, which spurred the building of supertankers able to ship oil around Africa.
Oil markets would feel it more if unrest spread elsewhere, as this might affect exports, and also spur hoarding
in anticipation of rising prices.
Against that, though, oil inventories are high, one reason why U.S. oil prices trade at big discounts to Brent
crude. And Saudi Arabia's oil minister re-emphasized Monday his country's spare capacity. Saudi Arabia's
willingness to use this helped quickly erase the doubling of oil prices after Iraq's 1990 invasion of Kuwait.
A price spike also could threaten fragile Western economies. China, meanwhile, seeing how inflation has
fueled Egypt's unrest, would have even more reason to crack down on rising prices, potentially curbing
energy demand.
Barring an extreme event like regime change in Saudi Arabia, oil, which has risen sharply already, could
prove a volatile hedge. Gold may make more sense. Apart from its traditional safe haven identity, gold has
become highly negatively correlated with real U.S. interest rates since the financial crisis. Gold stands to
suffer economic recoverygathers pace, as this brings nearer the day rates rise sustainably.
Higher energy prices hurting U.S. consumers actually could provide a rationale for extending lax monetary
policy for longer. In that environment, real gold could trump black gold.

Egypt's Contagion Risk
Heard on the Street
Egypt's Contagion Risk
By Richard Barley
305 words
1 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Egypt is sending investors a wake-up call about the risks in emerging markets.
For two years, investors have poured money indiscriminately into emerging-market assets that looked safe
relative to stocks and bonds from debt-laden developed economies. But declines in U.S., Asian and
European stocks and the rally in oil, bonds and gold reflect the risk that Egyptian unrest triggers contagion.
One risk is that the political turmoil spreads. Tunisia and Egypt are reminders of how volatile and
unpredictable emerging markets can be and that this needs to be reflected in their cost of capital. That could
trigger flows of capital out of emerging markets back into developed markets, already buoyed by the brighter
growth outlook for the U.S. Middle Eastern stock markets were hit hard over the weekend, and the cost of
insuring the debt of countries, including Morocco, Saudi Arabia, Qatar and Lebanon, has risen.
A second possible source of contagion is via the oil price, up 7.6% since Thursday. Investors are worried by
the risk of an extreme event like a closure of the Suez Canal or serious instability in big oil producers like
Libya or Saudi Arabia.
A third risk lies in the response to the high food prices that are a factor behind social tensions in North Africa
and the Middle East. That raises the possibility of policy errors. Monetary-policy makers might tighten too
quickly to stamp on inflation, or governments might try to ease food prices through subsidies, weakening
their fiscal positions.
Contagion will have winners as well as losers. Russia would benefit from higher oil prices; Brazil would gain
from higher agricultural and commodities prices. But even if Egyptian turmoil subsides, a lasting effect should
be greater discrimination between emerging-markets.

The Dow's Rockin' New Year --- Industrial Average Begins 2011 With Its Best January in More Than a Decade
Monday's Markets
The Dow's Rockin' New Year --- Industrial Average Begins 2011 With Its Best January in More Than a
Decade
By Jonathan Cheng
737 words
1 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Blue-chip stocks kicked off the new year with their strongest January in 14 years, as a boom in corporate
revenues and robust economic readings gave investors confidence that the U.S. economy has turned a
corner.
Helped by the Federal Reserve's open monetary spigot, stocks continued a rally that began in December.
Most of January was dominated by steady gains and few broad shocks, until protests erupted in Egypt last
week.
"It was in like a lamb, and out like a lion," said David Kelly, chief market strategist for J.P. Morgan Funds.
"January was a continuation of the trend we saw late last year -- what's changed is that the economy is
moving from recovery to expansion, and we're seeing a move up in various measures of consumer and
investor confidence."
On Monday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 68.23 points, or 0.6%, to close at 11891.93. The Dow's
2.7% gain for the month is the blue-chip index's strongest January since 1997 and the first positive January in
four years. Apart from last Friday's Egypt-related stumble, the Dow didn't shed more than 40 points on any
single day in January -- good news for market watchers who believe a strong January bodes well for the rest
of the year.
Still, several clouds loom on the horizon. For a start, some are worried the tensions in Egypt could spark a
contagion in other markets, setting off a spiral of selling. Others say Europe's troubles are far from over, and
many have their eyes turned to China, where the economy still is red hot. And in the U.S., some are
concerned recent revenue gains may not be enough to offset rising commodity prices, potentially crimping
corporate profits in coming quarters.
The prospect of rising commodity prices and inflation is a nagging worry for stock investors as they head into
February. Energy stocks were by far the strongest performers in January, as crude-oil prices broke above
$90 a barrel for the first time since the credit bubble. On Monday, Brent oil futures that are traded in London
finished above $101 a barrel for the first time since September 2008, and the U.S. benchmark, the West
Texas Intermediate, soared 3.2%.
Uncertainty in the Middle East and the prospect of further gains in oil prices could exacerbate the inflationary
pressures.
"Investors have seen enough evidence that the Middle East can throw a wild card -- it has for four decades,"
said Mr. Kelly of J.P. Morgan. "Investors would be right to be cautious here, and rising oil prices is simply a
reflection of that caution."
For now, many investors remain sanguine.
As the economic picture has improved, gold slid to its worst monthly finish since late 2008, falling as much as
$100 off the record high it notched at the beginning of the month. Gold finished January at $1333.80 an
ounce.
Even a return of European debt worries couldn't stop the Continent from boasting some of the best
performances among global stock indexes. Spain's IBEX 35 index rose 9.6%, while Italy's FTSE MIB index
rose 9.2%. Germany's DAX 30 index and France's CAC-40 index also finished the month just off their
respective 52-week highs.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the U.S., the month's gains were powered by economic improvement and a wave of fourth-quarter
earnings that showed corporate America beating revenue estimates at a level unseen since the recession,
bolstering hopes that the economy has begun to pivot out of recovery and into expansion.
Among Dow members, General Electric, International Business Machines and Exxon Mobil each added 10%
or more after announcing better-than-expected revenue and earnings numbers.
Each of the stocks is now trading at its highest in at least two years.
Still, rising costs, thanks to surging commodity prices, are giving some investors pause.
"Strong revenues are a reflection of a strong economy, and that's good," says Gary Flam, a portfolio manager
at Los Angeles-based Bel Air Investment Advisors, which manages $6 billion in assets.
"The warning sign is that, because of rising costs, the revenue beats have not been falling to the bottom
lines," Mr. Flam says.

A Cultural Conversation / With Jaume Plensa: The Man With a Thousand Faces
LEISURE & ARTS
A Cultural Conversation / With Jaume Plensa: The Man With a Thousand Faces
By Joel Henning
1,262 words
1 February 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
D5
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Chicago -- 'What is art?" the Barcelona and Paris-based sculptor Jaume Plensa asks me over breakfast at a
hotel hard by his monumental and iconic $17 million Crown Fountain in Chicago's Millennium Park. "Art
should be an echo," he continues, "a feeling of your heart . . . a mirror, a container of memory." At his Crown
Fountain, we see aspects of the childhood memories animating Mr. Plensa's work.
"I didn't grow up with art but with books," he says, "including medical books, but I had more of a fantastic than
a scientific interest. I loved the body. In fact I was obsessed by it."
No wonder, then, that the Crown Fountain's central aspect is a dramatic display of heads -- the heads of
1,000 Chicagoans filmed by the School of the Art Institute of Chicago (where he teaches courses on public
art). The heads randomly appear on huge LED screens covering two 50-foot-tall glass-block towers that
stand as massive bookends to a shallow black granite pool, fed by water cascading down the towers. At brief
intervals, each head puckers its lips and -- like a modern gargoyle -- spews a stream of water into the pool.
The 1,000 video images that dominate the fountain have inspired much of Mr. Plensa's subsequent work,
including another large video project for later this year in Seoul. This visit to Chicago is only a stopover on his
way to Calgary, Alberta, to oversee the selection of fabricators for his monumental public sculpture of a young
girl's head, planned for 2012.
Mr. Plensa's Calgary project follows from other recent public monuments featuring elongated faces of young
girls. One is "Dream" (2009). Fabricated in a luminescent finish of white marble and concrete aggregate, this
head of a young girl with her eyes closed rises more than 60 feet from the site of the former Sutton Manor
Colliery near Liverpool, England, shimmering above the skyline. His 16-foot-tall "Awilda" (2010), a bust of a
young girl in highly polished white Spanish marble, was unveiled in Salzburg, Austria, in October. Like
"Dream," she appears to spring out of the ground and cast a tranquil aura on her surroundings. Just as
"Dream" is intended to suggest hope for the future on the site of an abandoned coal mine, Mr. Plensa hopes
that the face of "Awilda" "injects an image of the future into the heart of our old traditions."
For his New York public-art debut in Madison Square Park on May 5, he is creating "Echo," a 44-foot-tall
head and face of a 9-year-old girl named for the goddess. "New York has so much movement. I want to do
something that doesn't move at all, but offers a still, quiet place to relax."
As we walk along Michigan Avenue, Mr. Plensa recalls other early memories. "I shopped with my mother in
Barcelona stores where we passed through beaded curtains. I thought the rustling of those curtains made
beautiful music. Also, I love not only great poetry but the physical aspect of text. So I dreamed about
transforming letters into something physical." In "Twenty Nine Palms" (2007), Mr. Plensa sculpted steel letters
of the alphabet and strung them together to hang -- like curtains in the Barcelona shops. The letters make up
selections from some of Mr. Plensa's favorite authors, including Baudelaire, Blake, Allen Ginsberg, Goethe
and Shakespeare. The work was exhibited earlier this year at Dallas's Nasher Sculpture Center. "Everybody
passed through these curtains, and the letters spoke to each other making beautiful music," he told me. "I
dreamed about transforming letters into something physical. Poetry transformed into music."
In his exhibition last fall at New York's Galerie Lelong, he included three sleeping female heads, all lit
internally with white neon and branded on their faces with words taken from "De Profundis," a letter from
Oscar Wilde to his paramour about the travails of prison life.
He uses letters "like cells of information" to create human forms, as in "I, you, she or he . . ." (2006), in the
permanent collection of the Frederik Meijer Gardens and Sculpture Park in Grand Rapids, Mich. Three
human figures sculpted of letters in stainless steel rest atop boulders, facing one another in what could be a
conversation. For the MIT sesquicentennial in Cambridge, Mass., he installed "The Alchemist," a human torso
more than 10 feet tall consisting of mathematical symbols in stainless steel.

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"As a child, when my father played piano, I was small enough to hide myself inside the case at the base of my
family's spinet. I could feel the vibrations," Mr. Plensa tells me. And this feeling of sound figured prominently
in the installation "Jerusalem" (2006), composed of 18 bronze gongs suspended at eye level, part of his
exhibition at the Meijer sculpture park last year. A padded mallet accompanied each gong, and viewers were
encouraged to strike the works. "You feel the vibration, then energy," Mr. Plensa says. For "World Voices"
(2010) in the lobby of Dubai's Burj Khalifa, the world's tallest building, he crafted 196 cymbals of stainless
steel, brass, bronze and gold. Here, water drips onto the cymbals, creating the interplay between sight and
sound.
Music is also central to the bronze self-portraits, each with arms and legs wrapped around living trees, on
exhibition outside of the Nasher Museum of Art at Duke University until the end of this week. Each is
inscribed with a composer's name, including Bartok, Beethoven and Gershwin. "I want people to remember
music as they look at them," Mr. Plensa explains.
"I invite people to touch the materials," Mr. Plensa says of his works. "So many things you have to explore
with your hands. I want people to be engaged, to touch. When you love somebody, you must embrace. When
I spoke in Des Moines . . . I invited people to touch my 'Nomade' [2007], a 26-foot-high stainless-steel figure
fabricated from latticed letters. But a lady told me that a sign said 'Do not touch.' I said to her, 'Then don't
touch the sculpture, caress it.' At my lecture at the Nasher Sculpture Center [in 2009], I said 'I'd love you to
touch the alabaster heads.' Even the guards touched them."
We return to the complexities he confronted with the Crown Fountain. "Crown was one of my most
challenging projects in a public space. It's like a symphony with many elements. The city took amazing risks.
They allowed me and my colleagues to create a technology mixing water, electricity and people."
When engineers announced that the project would cost about $17 million, well beyond what was originally
contemplated by the Crown family, the Crowns asked Mr. Plensa if he could scale it back. "I'm pretty good
with scale," he tells me he answered them. "I can reduce the scale of my piece if you can reduce the scale of
Chicago."
The Crowns supported the fountain on Mr. Plensa's scale. "They understood," he said to me. "We talk a lot
about weight and size, but not about soul. But it's about soul. Soul, not body. For the Crown Fountain, the
body is only the physical elements. The soul equals the faces. That's why you feel comfortable here." With
that, he left for Calgary to continue crafting his next face.

Mr. Henning writes about the arts and culture for the Journal.

Commodities: Mutual Fund or ETF?
Fund Track
Commodities: Mutual Fund or ETF?
By Sam Mamudi
507 words
31 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For all the talk about the success of exchange-traded funds, there is at least one sector where traditional
mutual funds maintain their supremacy: commodities.
Over the past year, commodity mutual funds have proportionally seen roughly five times the net inflows that
commodities ETFs have seen. Over the same period, stock mutual funds saw about three times the net
inflows of stock ETFs on a proportional basis.
One notable exception: the precious-metals subsector, where ETF flows significantly outstrip those of mutual
funds.
Data from fund-tracker Lipper Inc. show that 94 mutual funds in the broad commodities space saw $11 billion
in net inflows; 54 ETFs netted $1 billion.
The 25 precious-metals ETFs have seen close to $8 billion in net inflows; 74 mutual funds have reaped $3.7
billion.
The reason for broad commodity mutual funds' popularity, say professional investors, is largely that similar
commodities ETFs hold futures contracts. This leaves ETFs more prone to so-called contango effects, as well
as vulnerable to tax hits and front-running -- when traders can jump ahead of expected trades to profit from
subsequent demand.
Precious-metals ETFs avoid these problems by directly owning the underlying commodities.
Commodities ETFs usually hold futures contracts because the indexes they track are made up of contracts.
Thus ETFs have fallen victim to contango -- when a longer-term futures contact is more expensive than its
near-term counterpart. This hurts the fund because it means it loses money every time it moves from a
near-month contract to a further-dated contract.
One notable instance was when contango hit U.S. Natural Gas ETF (UNG) in 2009.
Mutual funds, such as Pimco Commodity Real Return Fund (PCRDX), typically gain exposure through debt
instruments such as swaps and prepaid forward notes, rather than futures, and so avoid the worst of these
problems.
Commodities ETFs also can suffer because of transparency -- the rollover dates of their contracts are known
by the market.
"The ETFs are so transparent that they were easily gamed," said James Shelton, chief investment officer of
Houston-based Kanaly Trust, which manages $1.6 billion for clients.
Mr. Shelton said he also prefers commodity mutual funds because many commodities ETFs are fairly new.
"Mutual funds have a better track record of performing across different markets," he said.
James Dailey, chief financial officer of Team Financial Managers, a Harrisburg, Pa.-based investment
adviser, noted that many of the new funds are still gathering assets, so liquidity can be a concern. Mr. Dailey,
whose firm manages more than $125 million, said that because commodities ETFs hold futures they also
have tax liabilities, of both the capital gains and mark-to-market variety.
The lack of futures contracts makes precious-metal ETFs alluring. These funds, including SPDR Gold Trust
(GLD), iShares Silver Trust (SLV) and iShares Gold Trust (IAU), actually hold the commodities rather than
futures contracts.

--- Page 144 ---
Page 144 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"It's as direct an investment as it can be," Mr. Shelton said, "short of buying gold itself."

Number of the Week: 52%
Optimal bank capital-to-assets ratio, Bank of England economists say.
Banks will need a lot more capital if we want to avoid financial disasters like the one that just shook the world,
according to the economists. Critics say that would hurt the economy by pushing up the cost of lending. But in
a new paper, the economists estimate that a permanent one-percentage-point reduction in the probability of
crisis is worth more than half a year's economic output, or about nine times the cost of permanently doubling
banks' capital ratios. Assuming that severe crises tend to come along every few decades or so, the optimal
capital level would be 52% of assets, weighted according to their riskiness. That compares to the 7%
requirement most big banks must meet by 2019.
-- Mark Whitehouse
