government warnings of global food supply shortages sent the prices of corn, soybeans and wheat higher.
Portugal's success in selling debt at yields that were lower than some investors had feared drove down the
cost of borrowing for fiscally stressed European nations. Yields on Portuguese, Spanish and Greek bonds all
dropped against benchmarks. In turn, Treasurys, German bunds and U.K. gilts declined.
U.S.-listed shares of Spanish banks Banco Santander and Banco Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria gained 12%
each.
Bank shares also fared well in the U.S. as financials led the gains after Wells Fargo upgraded its assessment
of the banking sector. J.P. Morgan Chase and Bank of America were among the best performers.
Driving optimism was the Portuguese government auction, which sold 1.25 billion euros ($1.62 billion) in
bonds at yields that were below market expectations. The auction offered a dose of good news after days of
mounting concern over Portugal's finances, with investors fretting that economic growth could be stunted if
governments are forced to enact tougher austerity measures.
"In 10 months, we've had two major countries go to the brink, and we've gotten by -- somehow, a deal is
struck each time," said Christian Thwaites, chief executive of Sentinel Investments. "The market is feeling
that we're going to muddle through, even if it may not be very pretty."
Still, some investors are bracing for further problems in Europe.
"What they're doing with Portugal is an extension of what they did with Ireland and Greece -- they're not really
fixing the core problem, they're buying time," said Paul Simon, chief investment officer for Tactical Allocation
Group in Birmingham, Mich.
The market also was responding to another step up in U.S. import prices in December, rounding out their
biggest three-month gain in over a year on the back of resurgent energy prices.
The price of imported goods rose 1.1% in December, following an upwardly revised 1.5% increase in
November, versus an initial estimate of 1.3%. The numbers suggest that fears of inflation could begin to
creep into the market, ahead of producer and consumer price index numbers in the coming days.
"Clearly energy and food are going higher, and that's directly related to what's going on in the commodity
market," said Kenneth Polcari, managing director at Icap Corporates. "That's something to be concerned
about, with oil pretty close to $100 now . . . the public is feeling it a lot more right now than the Fed is willing
to admit right now."

Oil, Copper Go Higher
Copper jumped 1.5%, while oil added 0.8% to settle at $91.86 a barrel. Gold edged up as well.
Treasurys sold off, pushing the yield on the benchmark 10-year note higher to 3.353%. Bond yields move
inversely to prices.
An auction of 10-year notes was well-received, with bids coming in at rates unseen since April.
Among stocks in focus, oil major Chevron gained 0.7% to 92.45 after saying late Tuesday that its
fourth-quarter earnings are expected to be higher than the profit reported in the preceding quarter.
American International Group fell 1.1% to 58.40 after the giant insurer decided to sell its Taiwan unit, Nan
Shan Life Insurance, to Taiwan's Ruen Chen Investment Holdings for $2.16 billion, another step forward in
the U.S. insurer's efforts to repay U.S. government bailout funds.
Shares of ITT surged 17% to 61.50 after the aerospace and defense firm said its board has backed a plan to
split the company into three publicly-traded companies.

BlackRock Gold ETF Wins Fans on Its Fees
BlackRock Gold ETF Wins Fans on Its Fees
By Tatyana Shumsky
627 words
13 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
BlackRockInc.'s iShares Gold Trust fund charges a 0.25% annual management fee. A Jan. 13 article about
the fund incorrectly said the fee was monthly.
(WSJ February 4, 2011)
(END)
Investors eager to snap up gold worth millions of dollars are flocking to a fund where savings are measured
in fractions of a penny per share.
BlackRock Inc.'s iShares Gold Trust (trading symbol IAU) has boosted its physical gold holdings by 36.9% in
the six months since undercutting its competitors' management fees. SPDR Gold Shares (GLD), the oldest
and by far the biggest gold exchange-traded fund, saw its gold volume shrink by 3.6% over the same period.
"It's always hard to know why money goes in or out . . . but it's true that we've had good inflows since we've
made these changes," said BlackRock spokeswoman Christine Hudacko.
Investors have flocked to gold as a safe-harbor investment during periods of economic uncertainty and
inflation fears. The exchange-traded funds allow investors to benefit from rising gold prices without having to
transport, store or insure the metal. But with such a simple investment formula -- buy gold and hold it --
newer funds have found it difficult to escape SPDR Gold's shadow. Cutting management fees is one of the
only ways to stand out.
"There are some gaps in expense ratios between identical products that are wide enough to drive a truck
though them," said Michael Johnston, senior analyst at ETF Database, an online provider of ETF research.
BlackRock's fund charges a 0.25% monthly management fee, while the standard for SPDR Gold and many
other similar ETFs is 0.4%. A $10,000 investment in a gold ETF at the start of December would have been
valued at about $10,240 by the end of the month based on gold's performance. The iShares ETF would
charge that investor about $2.10 in management fees at the end of December, compared to around $3.40
charged by the GLD.
For multimillion-dollar investors in gold ETFs, every fee cut translates into thousands of dollars in savings.
The widely watched Soros Fund Management, founded by billionaire investor and philanthropist George
Soros, added a five-million-share stake in BlackRock's ETF in the third quarter, while cutting its SPDR Gold
holdings by over half a million units, to around 4.7 million shares.
On Dec. 14, BlackRock's ETF saw an influx of around $369 million, the equivalent of 270,000 troy ounces of
gold and the fund's biggest single-day inflow in five years.
"Where it starts to matter is for the hedge fund and for the institutional investor who has $100 million in the
gold ETF, where we're dealing with significant dollar amounts," Mr. Johnston said.
The savings also can add up for smaller investors who hold an ETF for many months.
However, SPDR Gold still dwarfs its competition, with $56.3 billion in assets, over 10 times the holdings of
BlackRock's gold ETF, the next largest.
"GLD remains the perfect choice for institutional and retail investors," said Marie McGehee, spokeswoman
with State Street Global Advisors, the marketing agent for SPDR Gold.

--- Page 188 ---
Page 188 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The SPDR Gold ETF has advantages over competitors with lower management fees, such as cheaper
transaction costs, said Tom Anderson, head of ETF Strategy and Research at State Street Corp. Each SPDR
Gold share buys 10 times as much gold as a unit of the iShares fund, so an investor would need to buy or
sell fewer shares of GLD. Those costs can add up for investors charged for each share traded by their broker
or platform, Mr. Anderson said.

Investors Get in a Lather Over Tech --- Echoes of Dot-Com Craze Reverberate as Money Pours in to Start-Ups; One Big Difference: Profits
Investors Get in a Lather Over Tech --- Echoes of Dot-Com Craze Reverberate as Money Pours in to
Start-Ups; One Big Difference: Profits
By Jonathan Cheng, Pui-Wing Tam and Anupreeta Das
1,350 words
8 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It may be only a few days into 2011, but tech investors seem to be partying like it's 1999.
Wall Street, facing a raft of regulation and slower growth ahead, has relatively little to get excited about these
days. But financiers and investors alike are casting their gaze back to Silicon Valley, in what is shaping up as
an echo of the dot-com craze that minted millionaires on both coasts over a decade ago.
As a reminder of those euphoric times, the technology-oriented Nasdaq-100 index on Monday surpassed its
2007 highs to put the measure at its highest point since February 2001, the waning days of the dot-com
bubble. The Dow Jones Industrial Average, too, is within a hair's breadth of reclaiming the market peak it
reached in January 2000.
As investors show signs of warming to tech again, bankers have been racing to get there first, striking deals
with the Silicon Valley entrepreneurs driving the wave of innovation. This week, Goldman Sachs Group Inc.
led a $2 billion investment in Facebook Inc., the closely held social-networking company. That offering has
drawn a lot of interest.
The Facebook deal gives the company an implied value of $50 billion, five times its valuation in mid-2009 and
25 times revenue. Google Inc., by contrast, trades at a little over seven times revenue, about the same
valuation the search company fetched at the time of its IPO in August 2004.
Wednesday, people familiar with the matter said professional social-networking site LinkedIn Corp. moved
toward an initial public offering. Internet telephony company Skype also is working toward a public debut this
year. The daily deals website Groupon Inc. is raising $950 million, after already raising $175 million and
rejecting a $6 billion takeover offer from Google. The company also is preparing for a 2011 IPO, according to
people familiar with the matter.
Microblogging site Twitter Inc. said last month that it had raised $200 million in a funding that valued it at $3.7
billion, up from just $1 billion a year earlier.
The big money and heady valuations raise the question whether the miniboom in Internet companies may
presage a bust. Like the 1990s, some investors are buying in first and reading the fine print later, or not at all.
"Most sophisticated investment people think this is loony," said a Goldman client who has submitted a
request to participate in the Facebook deal without spending time analyzing the company's fundamentals. "I
think we are revisiting the 1998-1999 Internet mania."
Some said investor interest is different now. Most Web companies have something that was in scarce supply
in the bubble of the late 1990s: revenue and earnings. High valuations largely are confined to a small set of
Internet start-ups: Facebook, Twitter, Groupon and game company Zynga Inc. But they have amassed
millions of users world-wide that generate revenue.
"This isn't 1999," said Rory Driscoll, a venture capitalist at Scale Venture Partners in Foster City, Calif.
"Facebook is a singularity."
Another difference: Internet usage in the late 1990s still was in its relative infancy, especially broadband,
which hampered entertainment offerings like music and video. Today, broadband is more plentiful, and
smartphones are increasing access and usage.
Wall Street analysts aren't twisting themselves to justify heady valuations like they did during the dot-com
bubble, when it wasn't unheard of to value companies on what they spent, not what they earned.

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Web retailer Pets.com, which raised more than $110 million in capital and went public in early 2000, spent
$25 million on ads featuring a ballad-singing sock puppet. But by late 2000, the San Francisco company,
which offered deep discounts while incurring high costs from shipping items like 20-pound bags of dog food,
was struggling. It closed in November that year.
But around San Francisco's South of Market neighborhood, dozens of start-ups have sprung up in the orbit of
Twitter, Zynga and Yelp Inc. The race for good software engineers is heating up again, and enticements like
signing bonuses and free cafeterias are on the rise.
With interest rates low and good yield hard to find, some Silicon Valley veterans think the paucity of
promising investment options explains the frothy interest in Web companies.
"There are so few places to put money, other than gold and commodities," said Scott McNealy, the former
chief executive of Sun Microsystems Inc. The high valuations of some of the Web companies could drop fast,
he said. "Anything selling more than two times revenues implies Jack Welch is running the joint," he said,
referring to General Electric's former chief.
Evidence that there are too few high-tech IPOs to satisfy demand can be seen in the brisk trading of
private-company shares on the secondary market, said Salesforce.com Inc. Chief Executive Marc Benioff.
"The private markets are hotter than they ever have been," he wrote in an email. Shares of his
Internet-software company, which went public in 2004, are now trading at more than 250 times earnings.
The Nasdaq-100's 25-month rally from its late 2008 trough has driven Amazon.com Inc. and Apple Inc. to
records. Google is approaching a three-year high, and Netflix Inc.'s share price multiplied tenfold. Still, the
index's forward price/earnings ratio sits at about 16.9 times, far short of the 187 times multiple at the peak in
2000.
The Nasdaq's historic run-up in 1998-2000 initially followed a trend line similar to the current rally, until
rocketing higher in the last five months to peak at 313%, more than double the current 119% rally. As Birinyi
Associates points out, the comparison assumes the current rally has hit its peak.
As Wall Street spent the better part of 2010 cleaning up the mess of the financial crisis and bracing for the
Dodd-Frank regulatory overhaul, a frenzy of start-up investing was taking shape in Silicon Valley. Venture
capitalists, suffering from a decade of poor returns, searched for the next Facebook or Zynga.
Small "angel" investors, seeding companies with $25,000 of their own money, helped drive up the valuations
of young start-ups.
Some of the biggest venture-capital firms also piled in. Venture firm Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, which
didn't invest in Facebook and Twitter early on, in October launched the $250 million "sFund" to invest in
social-Web companies at a packed event at Facebook's Palo Alto, Calif., headquarters.
All this activity helped bigger Web companies, including Twitter and Groupon, to raise cash at high
valuations.
But it also has meant that some small companies with no revenue, and some with no product, got funding at
lofty valuations.
Those included Quora Inc., a question-and-answer website that in March raised about $14 million at a
valuation of about $87.5 million, people familiar with the matter have said. At the time, the company didn't
have a publicly available product and hadn't said how it will make money. The start-up, founded by former
Facebook executives, has since launched its website and continued generating buzz.
Adam D'Angelo, Quora's founder and CEO, said the company did get a "high valuation" but declined to be
specific, saying he thought the valuation "was reasonable."
The hype around the hot start-ups in recent months has led to "hundreds of Internet companies coming out of
the woodwork" to do an IPO, said one banker, "some of them with no revenue."
The improved liquidity has made it easier for venture capitalists to sell tech-company investments into the
public markets.
Some 46 venture-backed companies went public in 2010, generating $3.4 billion in proceeds, up from the
eight IPOs that raised $903 million in 2009, according to Dow Jones VentureSource.

World News: Sarkozy Likely to Seek Lesser Role for Dollar
World News: Sarkozy Likely to Seek Lesser Role for Dollar
By Nathalie Boschat and Ian Talley
579 words
8 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PARIS -- French President Nicolas Sarkozy is likely to make a provocative suggestion when he visits
President Barack Obama Monday: Give the dollar a less-prominent role in the world economy.
France has the rotating presidency of the Group of 20 industrialized and emerging nations this year, and Mr.
Sarkozy wants to use the platform to urge a rethinking of the international monetary system, with the aim of
reducing the consequences of excessive currency volatility on global growth.
Key to Mr. Sarkozy's goal is re-examining the role of the U.S. dollar and the Chinese yuan. Last year, Mr.
Sarkozy secured a commitment from China's President Hu Jintao that Beijing would participate in discussions
over currency issues. To get the initially reticent Chinese leader to the table, Mr. Sarkozy sidestepped the
touchy issue of the yuan's valuation.
On Monday, Mr. Sarkozy hopes to snag a similar commitment from Mr. Obama that he will participate in
multilateral currency talks. The argument Mr. Sarkozy will make is that overdependence on the dollar as the
de facto global reserve currency leaves nations exposed to policy decisions by the U.S. Federal Reserve over
which they don't have control, said an official close to Mr. Sarkozy.
"To talk about currencies, we need China and we need the U.S. around the table," another senior French
official said.
Economists and analysts say Mr. Sarkozy may be able to make small changes in the international monetary
system by highlighting the issue among G-20 states and by encouraging greater oversight over capital flows
by the International Monetary Fund. But they say it is unlikely he will be able to budge the dollar from its
global throne.
After World War II, when countries started moving away from the gold standard, the dollar gradually became
the reserve currency because the U.S. emerged as the dominant world power. Since then, international trade
has been in dollars, boosting the currency's use. The development of deep, liquid financial markets in the
U.S. and Washington's appetite for cheap debt have helped to cement the currency in its pre-eminent role.
Mr. Sarkozy believes that fostering a more "multipolar" currency system will also help address the increasing
thirst of fast-growing emerging nations for reserve assets, which the dollar alone can't quench.
The French leader said he wouldn't challenge the dollar's role, "which will no matter what remain prominent."
But, he added, "prominent doesn't mean exclusive."
A group of more than a dozen leading international economists led by a former French IMF chief is working
on recommendations to turn the president's thinking into an actual strategy.
The U.S. view is that it is merely supplying what the world demands; Washington sees little space for other
currencies to gain traction. Countries could choose another currency to stock reserves, but at a cost. And
there may not be enough supply of other currencies to meet some countries' needs.
The Fed's recent decision to buy $600 billion of U.S. bonds to boost the U.S. economy triggered ire in many
emerging countries as it accelerated flows of "hot money" into their economies. Those angry reactions to the
move could help Mr. Sarkozy advance the cause of a more multipolar currency system when he meets Mr.
Obama.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Common Sense: Betting Against the Conventional Wisdom in the New Year
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Common Sense: Betting Against the Conventional Wisdom in the New Year
By James B. Stewart SmartMoney
818 words
8 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Welcome to 2011, which already is giving investors a warm and fuzzy feeling, with stock markets in rally
mode and averages hitting post-recession highs. Indeed, the feelings are a little too rosy for this column,
which has often been wary of the conventional wisdom.
This year, the CW is mostly buoyantly optimistic. It may well be right. But if it is, profit opportunities will be
limited. It is when the CW turns out to be wrong that investors can really cash in.
As usual, Common Sense doesn't make a practice of predicting the future, and the CW is all about predicting
it. No one has perfected such a skill, and yet the exercise is often fascinating, especially at the beginning of a
new year. And investing always involves some assumptions about the future, unknowable though it may be.
Last year, Common Sense proved unusually foresighted in this exercise, which emboldens me to venture
another assessment of the conventional wisdom for 2011 and where it might be wrong.
-- Stocks. After two years of double-digit gains, the CW is decidedly bullish, with many pundits again
predicting a 10%-plus rise in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index. There are solid reasons to be bullish,
with consumer spending and sentiment on the rise, industrial production humming and the Federal Reserve
still in an accommodating mode. Rarely have the stars been so aligned in favor of stocks.
Yet the pervasive bullishness makes me nervous, as it did last year at this time. I said then: "I expect at least
one correction of 10% or more this year, which will represent a buying opportunity." For 2011, I expect
another modest correction, and gains in the 8%-to-10% range by year end. This also may be the year when
large-cap stocks take the lead from small- and mid-caps, and when a value orientation begins to overtake
growth.
-- Interest rates. The CW is deafening that the historic post-Lehman Brothers bond rally is over, and that
interest rates are destined to rise in 2011. This makes me very skeptical. Where was this chorus in August
or October, when bonds actually were hitting new highs and interest rates were at historic lows? The
problem with the CW is that bonds have corrected already. Shares of the iShares Barclays Aggregate Bond
exchange-traded fund fell 2.6% over the last two months of the year, which by bond standards is a big move.
I concede that interest rates remain very low, and long term, they pretty much have only one way to go,
which is up. But I'll go out on a limb to say that interest rates will remain low for most of 2011, and that the
big correction in bond prices is already over. I still would be wary of long maturities, and stick to intermediate-
and shorter-term bonds and CDs. But I feel comfortable adding to bond holdings at current prices and yields.
At least investors know they aren't buying at a market peak.
-- Gold and commodities. Gold has been on a tear, hitting new highs during 2010, along with many other
commodities. The attendant publicity has only made it more popular, and it has taken pride of place in
locker-room chatter.
All of this makes me wary. Given its limited industrial uses, gold has traditionally functioned mainly as a safe
haven during times of turbulence and an inflation hedge. I trust the Fed when it says inflation is in check,
and I see no signs of imminent economic chaos despite the hand-wringing over the size of the federal deficit.
Most commodities other than gold are another matter, since they have industrial uses. As the global recovery
gains strength, I would focus on commodity producers rather than the commodities themselves. Energy also
has traditionally performed well at this stage of a recovery, and I've added to those positions. I also have
maintained or expanded my holdings of commodity ETFs.
-- Foreign stocks. After surpassing U.S. stocks for several years, international equities trailed the U.S. last
year, and the CW is that the bloom is off foreign markets, especially China. Chinese equities were volatile in
2010, and the iShares FTSE China 25 Index Fund ended the year with a modest gain of just over 5%.

--- Page 195 ---
Page 195 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
I concede that the CW is much less pronounced here, but my sense is that foreign markets remain attractive,
especially China. I also expect developed Europe to emerge from the debt crisis, with Germany taking center
stage.
---
James B. Stewart, a columnist for SmartMoney magazine and SmartMoney.com, writes weekly about his
personal investing strategy. Unlike Dow Jones reporters, he may have positions in the stocks he writes about.
For his past columns, see: www.smartmoney.com/commonsense.

The Fed Should Have a Mandate It Can Likely Fulfill
Letters to the Editor
The Fed Should Have a Mandate It Can Likely Fulfill
706 words
6 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Marc Sumerlin's "The Fed's Dual Mandate Is Not the Problem" (op-ed, Dec. 28) shows that by concentrating
on a relative few critical inputs (consumer inflation and unemployment), our central bank has made a
muddle of monetary policy over the last 15 years. By what leap of logic should one assume that a central
bank with only two mandates, which it unsuccessfully met, will be able to better manage an even broader
mandate? How do we define the "skill" referred to at the end of the article? If we define skill as avoiding
current pain at the expense of more pain later, then Alan Greenspan exhibited great skill.
The real issue here is whether a central bank can "control" the natural rhythms of the business cycle. As
ugly as they might be anecdotally, economic troughs fulfill an important purgative function that must take
place in one manner or another. The siren's call of the ability of a central agency to level out the peaks and
valleys of a healthy, expanding economy has led to an unnatural state by which the Federal Reserve is
expected to prop up the economy. The use of props (low interest rates, as one example) inhibit the natural
selection process of the economy, and, like floods on the Mississippi River, the more we try to control the
natural state, the higher the price when the water eventually rises.
John Sirianni
Eau Claire, Wis.
---
The Federal Reserve created the circumstances that led to the mortgage bubble by keeping interest rates
artificially low to blunt the recession that followed the collapse of the tech bubble and 9/11. It continues to
follow zero-interest-rate policies to enable the banks to recover the losses incurred during the collapse.
Contrary to Mr. Sumerlin's analysis, current efforts by Rep. Paul Ryan and other Republicans in Congress to
reform the Fed's mandate would address these mistakes.
The Fed has defined "price stability" in a way that is inherently inflationary. The Fed measures inflation by
reference to the consumer price index, which has led it to ignore inflation in types of prices not included in
the consumer price index (e.g., the price of residential real estate and commodity prices). Second, the
informal inflation target has been set at 2% per annum, which means that the Fed has defined "stable
prices" to mean inflation of nearly 25% each decade. This bias arises from an inordinate fear of mild price
deflation. Historically, however, economic growth has continued during periods of mild deflation, such as
during the later years of the 19th century.
From January 2000 to January 2010, the price of gold went from $284 to $1,117 per ounce, oil went from
less than $23 to $74 per barrel and copper went from $1,670 to $6,980 per ton. Real-estate prices
experienced an unprecedented bubble, although they subsequently collapsed. Most significantly, the value of
the dollar fell against other major currencies, despite investors' growing preference for the relative political
stability of the U.S.
All of these trends distorted the investment environment, undercutting investments in improved productivity
and encouraging speculation based on the expected changes in asset prices in response to a depreciating
dollar. All of these developments were fed by easy money created by the Fed. Yet during this period, by the
Fed's measure, it was satisfying its mandate to maintain stable prices.
The solution to the inflationary bias is to narrow and clarify the Fed's mandate. That mandate should be to
maintain the value of the dollar. In addition, we should limit the Fed's responsibilities to those that enable it to
implement that mandate. The recent financial reform law goes in the opposite direction, giving the Fed a
greater responsibility than it had before for managing the financial sector. We need to move the Fed away
from the impossible task of "managing the economy" and toward the achievable goal of maintaining the value
of the currency.

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If the Federal Reserve managed to maintain a stable dollar, our underlying economic problems would for the
most part be resolved by markets.
Tom Kelly
Minneapolis

Page 198 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

Anglers Are in Dire Straits Along Istanbul's Bosphorus --- Mass Fish Migration Celebrated by Aristotle Has Slowed; Why Tuna Steered Right
Anglers Are in Dire Straits Along Istanbul's Bosphorus --- Mass Fish Migration Celebrated by Aristotle
Has Slowed; Why Tuna Steered Right
By Marc Champion
1,156 words
5 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ISTANBUL -- Every fall, at least for the last couple of thousand years, people here have been going nuts for
fish.
The cause is an annual mass migration, which has been late this year, of finned creatures as they head south
from the cooling waters of the Black Sea toward the warmth of the Aegean. To make the journey, however,
the bluefish, bonito, sea bass, horse mackerel and other species have to crowd from the open sea into the
tiny Bosphorus strait, just 820 yards wide at its narrowest, past an estimated 10,000 waiting rods and nets on
any given day.
Anglers, from women in head scarves to men in wool suits, pack so tightly along the Bosphorus that on some
stretches they're less than a yard apart. Adults shoo kids from the boardwalk because of the risk of getting
snagged. Anglers routinely pull up 10 little fish at a time on their multiple-hook lines.
"We call it the Central Bank," says Mustafa Kokos, head of one of the main commercial fishing co-operatives
on the Bosphorus, referring to the narrow throat of the 20-mile strait.
Back before the Turks conquered Constantinople in 1453, and then renamed it Istanbul, the Byzantines
thought so, too. Their empire stamped coins with the image of a bonito. The Golden Horn, the famed estuary
that leads off the Bosphorus under the city's ancient walls, was likely named "Golden" because of the
quantities of tuna caught during the three-month fall migration, historians say.
Aristotle thought the tuna hugged the right shore because they saw better with their right eye. (They do steer
to the right, but their left eyes are just fine). Homer, in the Iliad, had Agamemnon offer Achilles the riches of
the Bosphorus fishing grounds among other treasures as a bribe to keep the hero fighting at Troy.
Today, a micro-world of commerce revolves around even the small-time anglers who throng along the Galata
bridge, across the Golden Horn. Some man stations in tag teams, around the clock, selling what they catch to
passersby. Carpenters sell the anglers rod holders to strap onto the bridge railings; kebab, tea and corn
vendors feed them; bait sellers bring worms in buckets from a sewage-processing pond outside the city.
But the Central Bank is running out of gold. Mahir Ersin, who fishes for small quantities in his tiny boat says
that while little fish are abundant, the tuna are long gone, as are the swordfish, large bonito and Atlantic
mackerel that used to make the run when he fished here with his father in the 1950s and '60s. "We used to
catch 300-500 kilos of mackerel in a day," recalls the 65-year-old Mr. Ersin. Now, there are none.
Mr. Ersin, a retired journalist for state television, blames overfishing by people such as Mr. Kokos,
commercial fishermen who use sonar fish finders and large circular nets that close like a bag from
underneath. These, says Mr. Ersin, catch babies and mature fish alike. He's leading a web-based campaign
to tighten restrictions on what can be caught in the strait and where the big boats can fish.
"They aren't fishermen" but retired civil servants with a hobby who contribute nothing to the economy, Mr.
Kokos says of people like Mr. Ersin. "They want all these regulations on the Bosphorus and then they sit
around and talk and drink raki." He denied that the commercial boats catch undersize fish.
The dispute digs deep into ancient rivalries. Mr. Kokos calls his opponents "Greeks in Turkey." Unwittingly, he
says, they are helping Greek fishermen to catch the fish once they make it into the Aegean, passing the
Dardanelles strait. "The fish we catch here are migrating. If we don't catch them, then the Greeks and Italians
will," he says.
The European Union is another Trojan Horse, according to Mr. Kokos. If Turkey were to join the bloc, the EU
would demand as a condition of entry that it ban commercial fishing in the Bosphorus altogether and slash

--- Page 200 ---
Page 200 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the number of fishing businesses from 18,000 to 4,000, he says. Mr. Kokos doesn't want Turkey to join the
EU.
"They're basically saying, we will take you into the common market, and meanwhile we'll give 100 billion
euros ($133 billion) in aid to the Greeks and you can buy your fish from the Greeks," he says. A
spokeswoman for the European Commission said the EU hasn't yet finalized a negotiating position, because
the fisheries element of the talks are blocked because of a dispute over Cyprus.
"There's enough fish in Turkey to feed the world," says Mr. Kokos, who is also fisheries adviser to Prime
Minister Recep Tayyip Erdogan. He says he recently put a proposal to Mr. Erdogan to develop Turkey's
annual fish exports to 5 billion euros in five years from 180 million euros today, in part by keeping the
processing in Turkey. "We should put three hamsi (Black Sea anchovies) in a can, squirt some sauce on it
and sell it to the Americans."
If the big fish aren't crossing the Bosphorus anymore, that's not because of overfishing, but because of the
pollution dumped into the sea by a city that has grown more than 10 times in size since 1955, Mr. Kokos
says. Lights also disturb the fish, as do the churning propellers of the 55,000-plus tankers, container ships
and other large vessels that steam through the Bosphorus every year. And what about those 10,000 rod
anglers, he says: "If each one of them catches just one kilo, that's 10 tons of fish!"
All true, says Saadet Karakulak, professor of fisheries science at Istanbul University. But the biggest threat?
"Overfishing," she said, while acknowledging that, due to lack of funding, no research has been done to prove
it.
The fish most prized here is the lufer, a mature, bluefish that doesn't have the intensely oily flavor that causes
many Americans to throw the lufer's bigger Atlantic cousins back in the water. This year, the lufer haven't
come at all. Mr. Kokos and other fishermen are still waiting, hoping that the migration was just delayed
because the weather was too warm right up to early December. Mr. Ersin believes the lufer migration may be
going the way of the mackerel, because too many less-mature bluefish, known as cenekop and sarikanat
according to their sizes, are being caught. Mr. Kokos is having none of it.
"We heard about a Romanian ship that pulled up a ton of lufer in a single net," he said ruefully. Turkish boats
aren't allowed to fish in Romanian waters.
---
Ayla Albayrak contributed to this article.

Dollar Gets A Bump Up From the Fed
Currency Trading
Dollar Gets A Bump Up From the Fed
By Stephen L. Bernard
300 words
5 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar rallied broadly against most other major currencies Tuesday following fresh
indications that the pace of U.S. economic growth is accelerating, underscored by minutes from the Federal
Reserve's last policy meeting.
The U.S. government said factory orders rose unexpectedly in November, and the Federal Reserve
acknowledged the economic rebound seen in data in recent weeks should continue.
A sharp retreat in commodity prices, particularly gold, copper and oil, also drove down commodity-linked
currencies, helping the dollar.
David Gilmore, partner at Foreign Exchange Analytics in Essex, Conn., said investors were unwinding what
he termed "black swan" trades in currencies and gold Tuesday, following the new signs of economic
expansion.
The trades came last fall when investors thought the Fed was "driving into the land of Zimbabwe by printing
money and courting hyperinflation," Mr. Gilmore said. "All of the positions that grew out of the financial crisis
are going to start to come off . . . as the U.S. economy is generating some sense of normalization." A
significant unwinding of such trades could support the dollar.
Late Tuesday in New York, the euro was at $1.3297 from $1.3351 late Monday. The dollar was at 82.04 yen
from 81.74 yen, while the euro was at 109.09 yen from 109.12 yen. The pound was at $1.5585 from $1.5473.
The dollar was at 0.9490 Swiss francs from 0.9344 francs.
A stronger economy could drive Treasury yields higher, making the dollar more attractive, analysts said.
Factory orders jumped by 0.7%, far better than the 0.1% decline analysts had forecast. The report came a
day after news that growth in the broader manufacturing sector accelerated in December.

Gold Inches Up to Another High
Commodities Report
Gold Inches Up to Another High
By Matt Whittaker
354 words
4 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold eked out a record after struggling in negative territory for most of the day, and silver climbed to a more
than 30-year high, as positive economic sentiment shined differently on the two metals.
Investors held off on gold purchases as markets for stocks and other commodities rallied on the first trading
day of 2011. Market participants said the optimism, powered by positive U.S. data, dulled gold's attraction as
a safe-haven investment.
Thinly traded gold for January delivery gained $1.50, or 0.1%, to $1,422.60 a troy ounce on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. Actively traded February gold also rose $1.50, to $1,422.90,
also a record. The yellow metal rose for the second consecutive session, and has been up for five of the past
six.
Silver's gains outpaced those of gold. The metal is more widely used in industrial applications like electronics
than is gold. Comex January silver gained 18.60 cents, or 0.6%, to settle at $31.0960 an ounce, its highest
close since March 1980.
Gold lagged behind other commodities because it's "too high to buy at these prices with the stock market
acting well," said George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose to its highest close since August 2008. U.S. manufacturing data for
December showed improvement in the sector. Separately, U.S. construction spending in November rose for
the third consecutive month.
Investors often turn to gold when they are fretful about the economy, and they buy stocks when they are
willing to take on more risk. They have become more confident after the U.S. economy avoided sliding into a
second recession last year.
Absent any dismal economic data, gold probably will stabilize for a while around the $1,400 mark as
investors become wary of piling money into a precious metal with limited industrial use and zero yield, Mr.
Gero said. However, enough caution will remain that a selloff is unlikely, he said.

Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2010 --- Recalling a Year of Triumph and Tragedy
Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2010 --- Recalling a Year of Triumph and Tragedy
By Brian Hershberg and Michael Driscoll
7,394 words
3 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
R10
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Looking back on 2010, one could choose to dwell on the negative. There was no shortage of it.
In January, an earthquake devastated Haiti, killing more than 200,000 in the poorest country in the Western
Hemisphere. In April, a mining blast in West Virginia killed more than two dozen in the worst coal-mine
accident in the U.S. in 40 years. Also in April, and continuing through the summer months, an oil rig exploded
and collapsed in the Gulf of Mexico, spilling millions of barrels.
Juxtaposed against the misery, the human spirit shined. In January, the world gathered for the Winter
Olympics, where a luger lost his life just before the start; the Games went on and the host Canadians took the
gold in their most popular sport, hockey. Iraqis and Afghans took part in free, if violence-marred, elections.
Spain won its first World Cup. And in August, a collapsed mine shaft trapped 33 in Chile; less than three
months later a captivated world watched as every miner emerged, safe, from the darkness.
Also making headlines on the domestic and world stages: The U.S. Congress passed overhauls of health
care and financial regulation. Iran continued to press its nuclear ambitions. North Korea clashed with South
Korea. Europe's sovereign-debt crisis resulted in two major rescues and roiled the continent. Several terror
plots fizzled or were foiled. The "flash crash" shook markets.
Here is a summary of news events from the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most cases, the dates
reflect when the items were published, not the date of the event.
January
4 U.S. consumers in 2009 spent more on movie tickets than for buying movies to watch at home for the first
time since 2002. "Avatar" topped the $1 billion mark in global sales, the fifth film at that level.
5 Novartis said it will pay Nestle $28.1 billion as part of its bid to acquire U.S. eye-care company Alcon, the
biggest takeover in Swiss history.
A suicide bomber who killed seven CIA employees and contractors and a Jordanian in Afghanistan was a
double agent recruited by the CIA.
Dubai opened the world's tallest skyscraper, at 2,717 feet.

founder Raj Rajaratnam paid a tipster for inside information. The case continued throughout the year.
7 GM's chief said the auto company would make money in 2010, a bold prediction for a business that exited
bankruptcy in summer 2009.
11 Jay Leno will reclaim his 11:35 p.m. time slot as NBC abandons its prime-time experiment, touching off a
public-relations fiasco with "The Tonight Show" host Conan O'Brien.

Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2010 --- Meet the Supporting Cast --- Markets Continued to Benefit From Intervention in 2010; Now, the...
Year-End Review of Markets & Finance 2010 --- Meet the Supporting Cast --- Markets Continued to
Benefit From Intervention in 2010; Now, the Question Is, Can the Fed and Others Exit Neatly?
By Tom Lauricella
1,567 words
3 January 2011
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2011 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Financial markets of just about every stripe pushed higher in 2010, marking a second year of recovery from
the financial crisis.
As was the case in 2009, investors can thank continued, unprecedented efforts by governments and central
banks around the globe to keep their economies and financial markets afloat for those good returns.
At some point, governments will look to reverse stimulative strategies as their economies and markets can
stand on their own. But for now the betting is that in the U.S., Europe and Japan, at least, that support will
continue for months to come.
The benefits to investors could be seen in 2010. When major stock or bond markets faltered and ostensibly
signaled alarm, policy makers responded aggressively with measures that, at least in the short term, directly
or indirectly drove prices higher across a wide variety of asset classes.
The most direct support came as central banks supported government bond markets in the U.S. and Europe.
That then indirectly lifted corporate bond and stock markets.
U.S. stocks also got a boost in December thanks to an unexpected round of fiscal stimulus for 2011 in the
form of extended and new tax breaks. The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 11% to 11577.51, regaining
levels last seen before the collapse of Lehman Brothers in September 2008. The Standard & Poor's
500-stock index, meanwhile, rose 12.8% to finish at 1257.64. The Dow, which finished less than eight points
shy of its high for 2010, capped the year with a 7.3% gain in the fourth quarter and an 18.5% rise in the
second half. With those gains, the Dow stood 76.8% above its 12-year low close of 6547.05 hit on March 9,
2009, but is still 18% below its all-time high of 14164.53 reached Oct. 9, 2007
Prices of economically sensitive commodities climbed as central banks in many emerging markets held off
raising interest rates despite strong growth and building inflation pressures for fear of damaging the fragile
global recovery. Copper prices, for example, rose 33% and crude-oil prices gained 15% to finish the year
above $91 per barrel. The bet was that as long as China and other raw-material-hungry countries keep
growing at a fast pace, their demand would provide a bid for commodity prices.
In another indirect effect of stimulus efforts, investors worried about long-term damaging effects, especially
the possibility of higher inflation and exploding budget deficits, and bid up gold prices to record highs. Gold
rose $325.90 per troy ounce last year, or 29.8%, to $1421.10.
"The markets and economies aren't being left to their own devices," says Jason DeSena Trennert, chief
investment strategist at Strategas Research Partners. "Policy makers are trying to move heaven and earth."
This isn't quite the way many investors had expected 2010 to play out. As the year got under way, many
investors were pondering how well the markets would stand on their own as the rescue efforts launched in
late 2008 were pared back.
Now, as 2011 begins, the talk of "exit strategies" is concentrated only on the emerging markets, where some
central banks, such as those in China and South Korea, are starting to ramp up tightening efforts.
Among the world's biggest developed economies -- the U.S., Japan and much of Europe -- the consensus is
that support efforts will continue. That should be good news for riskier investments, such as stocks, high-yield
bonds and commodities.
For now, "very few investors are talking about unwinding" stimulus, says Erin Browne, macro strategist at
Citigroup. "Everyone sees it as an eventuality but it's not something that is on the near-term horizon."

--- Page 218 ---
Page 218 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The story of 2010 shows how government support continued to prop up financial markets. By the end of the
first quarter, U.S. stocks were on an upswing as solid earnings and improving economic news helped the
Dow in April top the 11000 mark for the first time since September 2008.
Then, in early May, the European debt crisis began to boil over from Greece. Contagion threatened other
euro-zone countries and European banks.
With turmoil spreading in the European bond markets, European governments unveiled a bailout package for
Greece on May 9 aimed at stabilizing financial markets. The staunchly independent European Central Bank,
meanwhile, bought some 40 billion euros of government bonds in the open market during May.
Those efforts gave stocks and other markets a brief boost. By July, however, the Dow was down 7% and
back below 10000 amid fears that the U.S. economy was sliding back into recession led by renewed
weakness in housing and a faltering jobs market. But the Federal Reserve rode to the rescue.
The turning point for the year came in late August, when during a speech in Jackson Hole, Wyo., Fed
Chairman Ben Bernanke opened the door for a second round of so-called quantitative easing. Stocks again
began to march higher. In September, the Fed made it official: The central bank planned to pump $600
billion into the markets through purchases of U.S. Treasurys.
Quantitative easing is designed to inflate prices of financial assets. Through purchases of Treasurys, the Fed
is keeping bond yields low and displacing other investors. This forces them to buy riskier investments, such
as stocks or corporate debt, an effect that eventually filters through to the economy.
The rally was given a final boost for the year in mid-December thanks to another round of government
stimulus. While investors had been closely watching negotiations between the White House and Republicans
over an extension of Bush-era tax cuts, they were surprised by news of a 2% reduction in Social Security
payroll taxes for 2011 in addition to two more years of the Bush cuts.
"Eventually, of course, there will be a cost to all these efforts," Strategas's Mr. Trennert says. But for the time
being, investors are being rewarded for not fighting the tide. "Our phrase at the beginning of 2010 was 'bullish
until the bill comes due' and that's still our view."
The stimulus efforts, by raising economists' short-term expectations for U.S. growth even in the face of a very
weak housing market and stubborn long-term unemployment, has investors putting money back to the U.S.
stock market, says Arnab Das, managing director of market research and strategy at Roubini Global
Economics.
For most of 2010, global investors had been pulling money out of U.S. stocks. In the first 11 months of the
year, U.S. stock funds had suffered withdrawals totaling $22 billion. Meanwhile, investors poured a record
$80 billion into emerging-market stock funds and $34 billion into emerging-market bond funds, according to
EPFR, which tracks global bond funds.
But in December, the tide turned sharply for U.S. stocks. In just three weeks, investors pumped more than
$22 billion into U.S. stock funds, EPFR says.
"We think that process of portfolio reallocation has a bit further to run . . . and we're looking for a tactical
outperformance of equities" in 2011, Mr. Das says.
Still, Mr. Das says investors shouldn't ignore the reason for the support efforts. Fiscal and economic structural
problems are "at the heart of why we need so many Band-Aids," he says.
That means risks remain, especially in Europe during the first half of the year, Mr. Das and others say. "Event
risk in the euro zone remains very high," Mr. Das says. "Portugal's turn will come and there's better than even
odds that Spain, too, will be significantly tested in the coming couple of quarters."
There is a different dynamic in the emerging markets. Market support has come not from active intervention
but rather from caution in withdrawing stimulus even as those economies have recovered better than those in
Japan, Europe and the U.S. But that sentiment seems to be changing.
Facing growing inflation issues, "policy makers are looking to withdraw stimulus at a more rapid pace," says
Ramin Toloui, co-head of emerging markets at Pacific Investment Management Co. "The key thing for
emerging-market countries is going to be a measured withdrawal."
The question facing investors is how emerging-market governments and central bankers handle the task of
taking their foot off the gas at the same time as the accelerator is still being pushed in the U.S. Making the
task trickier could be higher commodity prices, which by fueling inflation, could force emerging markets to
tighten more aggressively. That, in turn, could spook other markets.

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 219 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Citigroup's Ms. Browne thinks the tightening efforts in emerging markets will weigh on stocks in those parts of
the world. "The U.S. market will still be bolstered by the easy liquidity provided by the Fed," she says.
The split between policies in the G-3 economies -- the U.S., Japan and Europe -- and emerging markets is on
the mind of many investors. Pimco's Mr. Toloui says: "Managing this multispeed world is going to be a key
theme for 2011."
(See related article: "Wall Street Warms to China Story" -- WSJ Jan. 3, 2011)
